<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;border-bottom:1px solid #aaa;line-height:1.5em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hole_in_flag_-_Budapest_1956.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Hole in flag - Budapest 1956.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Hole_in_flag_-_Budapest_1956.jpg/250px-Hole_in_flag_-_Budapest_1956.jpg" width="250" height="346" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Hole_in_flag_-_Budapest_1956.jpg/375px-Hole_in_flag_-_Budapest_1956.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/Hole_in_flag_-_Budapest_1956.jpg 2x" data-file-width="433" data-file-height="600" /></a><br />
<a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Hungary" title="Flag of Hungary">Flag of Hungary</a>, with the communist coat of arms cut out. The flag with a hole became the symbol of the revolution.</td>
</tr>

<td style="width:50%;padding-left:0.25em">2,500–3,000 killed <small>(est.)</small><br />

<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Eastern_Bloc_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>Hungarian Revolution of 1956</b> or the <b>Hungarian Uprising of 1956</b><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a>: <span lang="hu" xml:lang="hu"><i>1956-os forradalom</i></span> or <span lang="hu" xml:lang="hu"><i>1956-os felkelés</i></span>) was a nationwide <a href="/wiki/Revolt" class="mw-redirect" title="Revolt">revolt</a> against the government of the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_People%27s_Republic" title="Hungarian People's Republic">Hungarian People's Republic</a> and its <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a>-imposed policies, lasting from 23 October until 10 November 1956. Though leaderless when it first began, it was the first major threat to Soviet control since the USSR's forces drove out Nazi Germany from its territory at the end of World War II and broke into Central and Eastern Europe.</p>
<p>The revolt began as a student demonstration, which attracted thousands as they marched through central <a href="/wiki/Budapest" title="Budapest">Budapest</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Parliament_Building" title="Hungarian Parliament Building">Parliament building</a>, calling out on the streets using a van with loudspeakers via <a href="/wiki/Radio_Free_Europe/Radio_Liberty" title="Radio Free Europe/Radio Liberty">Radio Free Europe</a>. A student delegation, entering the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Radio" class="mw-redirect" title="Hungarian Radio">radio building</a> to try to broadcast <a href="/wiki/Demands_of_Hungarian_Revolutionaries_of_1956" title="Demands of Hungarian Revolutionaries of 1956">the students' demands</a>, was detained. When the delegation's release was demanded by the demonstrators outside, they were fired upon by the <a href="/wiki/State_Protection_Authority" title="State Protection Authority">State Security Police</a> (ÁVH) from within the building. One student died and was wrapped in a flag and held above the crowd. This was the start of the revolution. As the news spread, disorder and violence erupted throughout the capital.</p>
<p>The revolt spread quickly across <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Hungary" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Republic of Hungary">Hungary</a> and the government collapsed. Thousands organised into <a href="/wiki/Militia" title="Militia">militias</a>, battling the ÁVH and <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Army" title="Soviet Army">Soviet troops</a>. Pro-Soviet communists and ÁVH members were often executed or imprisoned and former political prisoners were released and armed. Radical impromptu <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_council" title="Workers' council">workers' councils</a> wrested municipal control from the ruling <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Working_People%27s_Party" title="Hungarian Working People's Party">Hungarian Working People's Party</a> and demanded political changes. A new government formally disbanded the ÁVH, declared its intention to withdraw from the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a>, and pledged to re-establish free elections. By the end of October, fighting had almost stopped and a sense of normality began to return.</p>
<p>After announcing a willingness to negotiate a withdrawal of Soviet forces, the <a href="/wiki/Politburo_of_the_Central_Committee_of_the_Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Politburo of the Central Committee of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union">Politburo</a> changed its mind and moved to crush the revolution. On 4 November, a large Soviet force invaded Budapest and other regions of the country. The Hungarian resistance continued until 10 November. Over 2,500 Hungarians and 700 Soviet troops were killed in the conflict, and 200,000 Hungarians fled as refugees. Mass arrests and denunciations continued for months thereafter. By January 1957, the new Soviet-installed government had suppressed all public opposition. These Soviet actions, while strengthening control over the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a>, alienated many Western <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxists</a>, leading to splits and/or considerable losses of membership for <a href="/wiki/Communist_party" title="Communist party">Communist Parties</a> in the West.</p>
<p>Public discussion about this revolution was suppressed in Hungary for more than 30 years. Since the thaw of the 1980s, it has been a subject of intense study and debate. At the inauguration of the <a href="/wiki/Third_Hungarian_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Hungarian Republic">Third Hungarian Republic</a> in 1989, 23 October was declared a national holiday.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prelude">Prelude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Prelude">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>During World War II Hungary was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis powers</a>, allied with the forces of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(1861%E2%80%931946)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Italy (1861–1946)">Fascist Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Romania" title="Kingdom of Romania">Romania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bulgaria" title="Kingdom of Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>. In 1941, the Hungarian military <a href="/wiki/Hungary_during_World_War_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Hungary during World War II">participated</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Yugoslavia" title="Invasion of Yugoslavia">occupation of Yugoslavia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">invasion of the Soviet Union</a>. The Soviet army was able to force back the Hungarian and other Axis invaders. By 1944 Soviet armies were advancing towards Hungary.</p>
<p>Fearing invasion, the Hungarian government began armistice negotiations with the Allies. These ended when <a href="/wiki/Operation_Margarethe" title="Operation Margarethe">Nazi Germany invaded and occupied</a> the country and set up its own pro-Axis regime, the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_National_Unity_(Hungary)" title="Government of National Unity (Hungary)">Government of National Unity</a>.</p>
<p>Both Hungarian and German forces stationed in Hungary were subsequently defeated when the Soviet Union invaded the country in 1945.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar_occupation">Postwar occupation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Postwar occupation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Towards the end of World War II, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Army" title="Soviet Army">Soviet Army</a> occupied Hungary, with the country coming under the Soviet Union's <a href="/wiki/Sphere_of_influence" title="Sphere of influence">sphere of influence</a>. Imediately after World War II, Hungary was a <a href="/wiki/Multiparty_democracy" class="mw-redirect" title="Multiparty democracy">multiparty democracy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_parliamentary_election,_1945" title="Hungarian parliamentary election, 1945">elections in 1945</a> produced a <a href="/wiki/Independent_Smallholders,_Agrarian_Workers_and_Civic_Party" title="Independent Smallholders, Agrarian Workers and Civic Party">coalition government</a> under Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Zolt%C3%A1n_Tildy" title="Zoltán Tildy">Zoltán Tildy</a>. However, the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Communist_Party" title="Hungarian Communist Party">Hungarian Communist Party</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Leninist" class="mw-redirect" title="Leninist">Marxist–Leninist</a> group who shared the Soviet government's ideological beliefs, constantly wrested small concessions in a process named <a href="/wiki/Salami_tactics" title="Salami tactics">salami tactics</a>, which sliced away the elected government's influence, despite the fact that it had received only 17% of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UNPara47_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNPara47-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the elections of 1945, the portfolio of the Interior Ministry, which oversaw the <a href="/wiki/State_Protection_Authority" title="State Protection Authority">Hungarian State Security Police</a> (<i>Államvédelmi Hatóság</i>, later known as the ÁVH), was forcibly transferred from the Independent Smallholders Party to a nominee of the Communist Party.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The ÁVH employed methods of <a href="/wiki/Intimidation" title="Intimidation">intimidation</a>, falsified accusations, imprisonment, and <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a> to suppress political opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The brief period of multi-party <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a> came to an end when the Communist Party merged with the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Social_Democratic_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Hungarian Social Democratic Party">Social Democratic Party</a> to become the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Working_People%27s_Party" title="Hungarian Working People's Party">Hungarian Working People's Party</a>, which stood its candidate list unopposed in 1949. The <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Hungary" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Republic of Hungary">People's Republic of Hungary</a> was then declared.<sup id="cite_ref-UNPara47_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNPara47-7">[7]</a></sup> By 1949, the Soviets had concluded a mutual assistance treaty, the <a href="/wiki/Comecon" title="Comecon">Comecon</a>, with Hungary, that granted the Soviet Union rights to a continued military presence, assuring ultimate political control.</p>
<p>The Hungarian Working People's Party set about to modify the economy into <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a> by undertaking radical <a href="/wiki/Nationalization" title="Nationalization">nationalization</a> based on the Soviet model. This forced method of economic socialisation during infrastructural recovery from the war initially resulted in economic stagnation, lower standards of living, and a deep malaise. Writers and journalists were the first to voice open criticism of the government and its policies, publishing critical articles in 1955.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> By 22 October 1956, Technical University students had resurrected the banned MEFESZ <a href="/wiki/Students%27_union" title="Students' union">student union</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Crampton295_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crampton295-11">[11]</a></sup> and staged a demonstration on 23 October that set off a chain of events leading directly to the revolution.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_repression_and_economic_decline">Political repression and economic decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Political repression and economic decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hungary became a <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Hungary" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Republic of Hungary">communist state</a> under the severely authoritarian leadership of <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A1ty%C3%A1s_R%C3%A1kosi" title="Mátyás Rákosi">Mátyás Rákosi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Under Rákosi's reign, the Security Police (<a href="/wiki/State_Protection_Authority" title="State Protection Authority">ÁVH</a>) began a series of purges, first within the Communist Party to end opposition to Rákosi's reign. The victims were labeled as "<a href="/wiki/Titoism" title="Titoism">Titoists</a>," "western agents," or "<a href="/wiki/Trotskyism" title="Trotskyism">Trotskyists</a>" for as insignificant a crime as spending time in the West to participate in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a>. In total, about half of all the middle and lower level party officials—at least 7,000 people—were purged.<sup id="cite_ref-Litv.C3.A1n_1996_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Litv.C3.A1n_1996-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tokes317_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tokes317-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wilson_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:R%C3%A1kosi_M%C3%A1ty%C3%A1s_fortepan_56324.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/R%C3%A1kosi_M%C3%A1ty%C3%A1s_fortepan_56324.jpg/170px-R%C3%A1kosi_M%C3%A1ty%C3%A1s_fortepan_56324.jpg" width="170" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/R%C3%A1kosi_M%C3%A1ty%C3%A1s_fortepan_56324.jpg/255px-R%C3%A1kosi_M%C3%A1ty%C3%A1s_fortepan_56324.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/R%C3%A1kosi_M%C3%A1ty%C3%A1s_fortepan_56324.jpg/340px-R%C3%A1kosi_M%C3%A1ty%C3%A1s_fortepan_56324.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2227" data-file-height="3170" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:R%C3%A1kosi_M%C3%A1ty%C3%A1s_fortepan_56324.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mátyás Rákosi speaks in Budapest, 1948</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From 1950 to 1952, the Security Police forcibly relocated thousands of people to obtain property and housing for the Working People's Party members, and to remove the threat of the intellectual and 'bourgeois' class. Thousands were arrested, tortured, tried, and imprisoned in <a href="/wiki/Concentration_camps" class="mw-redirect" title="Concentration camps">concentration camps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Population_transfer_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Population transfer in the Soviet Union">deported to the east</a>, or were executed, including ÁVH founder <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A1szl%C3%B3_Rajk" title="László Rajk">László Rajk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tokes317_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tokes317-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lawsoc_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lawsoc-16">[16]</a></sup> In a single year, more than 26,000 people were forcibly relocated from Budapest. As a consequence, jobs and housing were very difficult to obtain. The deportees generally experienced terrible living conditions and were interned as slave labor on <a href="/wiki/Collective_farms" class="mw-redirect" title="Collective farms">collective farms</a>. Many died as a result of poor living conditions and malnutrition.<sup id="cite_ref-wilson_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Rákosi government thoroughly politicised Hungary's educational system to supplant the educated classes with a "toiling intelligentsia".<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Russian language study and Communist political instruction were made mandatory in schools and universities nationwide. Religious schools were nationalized and church leaders were replaced by those loyal to the government.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In 1949 the leader of the Hungarian Catholic Church, Cardinal <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zsef_Mindszenty" title="József Mindszenty">József Mindszenty</a>, was arrested and sentenced to life imprisonment for treason.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Under Rákosi, Hungary's government was among the most repressive in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-UNPara47_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNPara47-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lawsoc_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lawsoc-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The post-war Hungarian economy suffered from multiple challenges. Hungary agreed to pay <a href="/wiki/War_reparations" title="War reparations">war reparations</a> approximating US$300 million to the Soviet Union, <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" title="Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a> and to support Soviet garrisons.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_National_Bank" title="Hungarian National Bank">Hungarian National Bank</a> in 1946 estimated the cost of reparations as "between 19 and 22 per cent of the annual national income."<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In 1946, the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_peng%C5%91" title="Hungarian pengő">Hungarian currency</a> experienced marked <a href="/wiki/Depreciation" title="Depreciation">depreciation</a>, resulting in the highest historic rates of <a href="/wiki/Hyperinflation" title="Hyperinflation">hyperinflation</a> known.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Hungary's participation in the Soviet-sponsored <a href="/wiki/COMECON" class="mw-redirect" title="COMECON">COMECON</a> (Council Of Mutual Economic Assistance) prevented it from trading with the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">West</a> or receiving <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> aid.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition, Rákosi began his first Five-Year Plan in 1950-based on <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>'s industrial program of the <a href="/wiki/Five-Year_Plans_for_the_National_Economy_of_the_Soviet_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Five-Year Plans for the National Economy of the Soviet Union">same name</a> that sought to raise industrial output by 380 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-Litv.C3.A1n_1996_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Litv.C3.A1n_1996-13">[13]</a></sup> Like its Soviet counterpart, the Five-Year Plan never achieved these outlandish goals due in part to the crippling effect of the exportation of most of Hungary's raw resources and technology to the Soviet Union as well as Rákosi's purges of much of the former professional class. In fact, the Five-Year Plan weakened Hungary's existing industrial structure and caused real industrial wages to fall by 18 percent between 1949 and 1952.<sup id="cite_ref-Litv.C3.A1n_1996_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Litv.C3.A1n_1996-13">[13]</a></sup> Rákosi's agricultural programs met with the same lack of success, with attempted collectivization of the peasantry causing a marked fall in agricultural output and a rise in food shortages.</p>
<p>Although national income per capita rose in the first third of the 1950s, the standard of living fell. Huge income deductions to finance industrial investment reduced <a href="/wiki/Disposable_and_discretionary_income" title="Disposable and discretionary income">disposable personal income</a>; mismanagement created chronic shortages in basic foodstuffs resulting in rationing of bread, sugar, flour, and meat.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Compulsory subscriptions to state bonds further reduced personal income. The net result was that disposable real income of workers and employees in 1952 was only two-thirds of what it had been in 1938, whereas in 1949, the proportion had been 90%.<sup id="cite_ref-transformation_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-transformation-25">[25]</a></sup> These policies had a cumulative negative effect and fueled discontent as foreign debt grew and the population experienced shortages of goods.<sup id="cite_ref-LibCongressHungaryEconomy_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LibCongressHungaryEconomy-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_events">International events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: International events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nagy_Imre_fortepan_74215.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Nagy_Imre_fortepan_74215.jpg/220px-Nagy_Imre_fortepan_74215.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Nagy_Imre_fortepan_74215.jpg/330px-Nagy_Imre_fortepan_74215.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Nagy_Imre_fortepan_74215.jpg/440px-Nagy_Imre_fortepan_74215.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8141" data-file-height="5601" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nagy_Imre_fortepan_74215.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Imre Nagy (center) in October 1956</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 5 March 1953, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> died, ushering in a period of <a href="/wiki/Destalinization" class="mw-redirect" title="Destalinization">moderate liberalization</a>, when most European communist parties developed a reform wing. In Hungary, the reformist <a href="/wiki/Imre_Nagy" title="Imre Nagy">Imre Nagy</a> replaced Rákosi, "Stalin's Best Hungarian Disciple", as Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> However, Rákosi remained General Secretary of the Party, and was able to undermine most of Nagy's reforms. By April 1955, he had Nagy discredited and removed from office.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> After <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Khrushchev's</a> <a href="/wiki/On_the_Personality_Cult_and_its_Consequences" class="mw-redirect" title="On the Personality Cult and its Consequences">"secret speech"</a> of February 1956, which denounced Stalin and his protégés,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Rákosi was deposed as General Secretary of the Party and replaced by <a href="/wiki/Ern%C5%91_Ger%C5%91" title="Ernő Gerő">Ernő Gerő</a> on 18 July 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Radio_Free_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Radio Free Europe">Radio Free Europe</a> would broadcast the "secret speech" to Eastern Europe on the advice of <a href="/wiki/Ray_S._Cline" title="Ray S. Cline">Ray S. Cline</a>, who saw it as a way to "as I think I told <a href="/wiki/Allen_Dulles" title="Allen Dulles">[Allen Dulles]</a> to say, 'indict the whole Soviet system'." <sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 14 May 1955, the Soviet Union created the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a>, binding Hungary to the Soviet Union and its <a href="/wiki/Satellite_state" title="Satellite state">satellite states</a> in Central and Eastern Europe. Among the principles of this alliance were "respect for the independence and sovereignty of states" and "non-interference in their internal affairs".<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1955, the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_State_Treaty" title="Austrian State Treaty">Austrian State Treaty</a> and ensuing declaration of neutrality established Austria as a demilitarised and neutral country.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> This raised Hungarian hopes of also becoming neutral and in 1955 Nagy had considered "...&#160;the possibility of Hungary adopting a neutral status on the Austrian pattern".<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>In June 1956, <a href="/wiki/Pozna%C5%84_1956_protests" title="Poznań 1956 protests">a violent uprising by Polish workers</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pozna%C5%84" title="Poznań">Poznań</a> was put down by the government, with scores of protesters killed and wounded. Responding to popular demand, in October 1956, the government appointed the recently <a href="/wiki/Political_rehabilitation" title="Political rehabilitation">rehabilitated</a> reformist communist <a href="/wiki/W%C5%82adys%C5%82aw_Gomu%C5%82ka" title="Władysław Gomułka">Władysław Gomułka</a> as First Secretary of the <a href="/wiki/Polish_United_Workers%27_Party" title="Polish United Workers' Party">Polish United Workers' Party</a>, with a mandate to negotiate trade concessions and troop reductions with the Soviet government. After a few tense days of negotiations, on 19 October the Soviets finally gave in to Gomułka's reformist demands.<sup id="cite_ref-satellite_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-satellite-35">[35]</a></sup> News of the concessions won by the Poles, known as <a href="/wiki/Polish_October" title="Polish October">Polish October</a>, emboldened many Hungarians to hope for similar concessions for Hungary and these sentiments contributed significantly to the highly charged political climate that prevailed in Hungary in the second half of October 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-Machcewicz_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Machcewicz-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Within the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> context of the time, by 1956, a fundamental tension had appeared in US policy towards Hungary and the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a> generally. The United States hoped to encourage European countries to break away from the bloc through their own efforts but wanted to avoid a US-Soviet military confrontation, as escalation might lead to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear war">nuclear war</a>. For these reasons, US policy makers had to consider other means of diminishing Soviet influence in Eastern Europe, short of a <a href="/wiki/Rollback" title="Rollback">rollback</a> policy. This led to the development of <a href="/wiki/Containment" title="Containment">containment</a> policies such as economic and psychological warfare, covert operations, and, later, negotiation with the Soviet Union regarding the status of the Eastern states.<sup id="cite_ref-BorhiContRoll_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BorhiContRoll-37">[37]</a></sup> Vice President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> had also argued to the National Security Council that it would serve US interests if the Soviet Union would turn on another uprising as they had in Poland, providing a source of anti-Communist propaganda.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> However, while CIA director <a href="/wiki/Allen_Dulles" title="Allen Dulles">Allen Dulles</a> had claimed he was creating an extensive network in Hungary, at the time the agency had no Hungarian station, almost no agents who spoke the language, and unreliable, corrupt local assets. The agency's own secret history would admit "at no time did we have anything that could or should have been mistaken for an intelligence operation".<sup id="cite_ref-Ashes_page_149_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashes_page_149-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the summer of 1956, relations between Hungary and the US began to improve. At that time, the US responded very favourably to Hungary's overtures about a possible expansion of bilateral trade relations. Hungary's desire for better relations was partly attributable to the country's catastrophic economic situation. Before any results could be achieved, however, the pace of negotiations was slowed by the Hungarian Ministry of Internal Affairs, which feared that better relations with the West might weaken Communist rule in Hungary.<sup id="cite_ref-BorhiContRoll_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BorhiContRoll-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_unrest_builds">Social unrest builds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Social unrest builds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Rákosi's resignation in July 1956 emboldened students, writers, and journalists to be more active and critical in politics. Students and journalists started a series of intellectual forums examining the problems facing Hungary. These forums, called <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1ndor_Pet%C5%91fi" title="Sándor Petőfi">Petőfi</a> circles, became very popular and attracted thousands of participants.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> On 6 October 1956, <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A1szl%C3%B3_Rajk" title="László Rajk">László Rajk</a>, who had been executed by the Rákosi government, was reburied in a moving ceremony that strengthened the party opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 16 October 1956, university students in <a href="/wiki/Szeged" title="Szeged">Szeged</a> snubbed the official communist student union, the DISZ, by re-establishing the MEFESZ (Union of Hungarian University and Academy Students), a democratic student organization, previously banned under the Rákosi dictatorship.<sup id="cite_ref-Crampton295_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crampton295-11">[11]</a></sup> Within days, the student bodies of <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A9cs" title="Pécs">Pécs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miskolc" title="Miskolc">Miskolc</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sopron" title="Sopron">Sopron</a> followed suit. On 22 October, students of the <a href="/wiki/Budapest_University_of_Technology_and_Economics" title="Budapest University of Technology and Economics">Technical University</a> compiled a list of <a href="/wiki/Demands_of_Hungarian_Revolutionaries_of_1956" title="Demands of Hungarian Revolutionaries of 1956">sixteen points</a> containing several national policy demands.<sup id="cite_ref-sixteen_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sixteen-42">[42]</a></sup> After the students heard that the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Writers%27_Union" title="Hungarian Writers' Union">Hungarian Writers' Union</a> planned on the following day to express solidarity with pro-reform movements in Poland by laying a wreath at the statue of Polish-born <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Bem" title="Józef Bem">General Bem</a>, a hero of the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Revolution_of_1848" title="Hungarian Revolution of 1848">Hungarian Revolution of 1848</a> (1848–49), the students decided to organize a parallel demonstration of sympathy.<sup id="cite_ref-Machcewicz_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Machcewicz-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolution">Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_shots">First shots</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: First shots">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On the afternoon of 23 October 1956, approximately 20,000 protesters convened next to the statue of <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zsef_Bem" class="mw-redirect" title="József Bem">József Bem</a>—a national hero of Poland and Hungary.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A9ter_Veres_(politician)" title="Péter Veres (politician)">Péter Veres</a>, President of the Writers' Union, read a manifesto to the crowd, which included: The desire for Hungary to be independent from all foreign powers; a political system based on democratic socialism (land reform and public ownership of some businesses); Hungary joining the United Nations; and citizens of Hungary should have all the rights of free men.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> After the students read their proclamation, the crowd chanted a censored patriotic poem the "<a href="/wiki/Nemzeti_dal" title="Nemzeti dal">National Song</a>", with the refrain: "This we swear, this we swear, that we will no longer be slaves." Someone in the crowd cut out the Communist coat of arms from the Hungarian flag, leaving a distinctive hole and others quickly followed suit.<sup id="cite_ref-Heller_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heller-46">[46]</a></sup> Afterwards, most of the crowd crossed the <a href="/wiki/River_Danube" class="mw-redirect" title="River Danube">River Danube</a> to join demonstrators outside the Parliament Building. By 18:00, the multitude had swollen to more than 200,000 people;<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> the demonstration was spirited, but peaceful.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_report_2C_p55_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_report_2C_p55-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Szt%C3%A1lin_szobor_Budapest_1956.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Szt%C3%A1lin_szobor_Budapest_1956.jpg/220px-Szt%C3%A1lin_szobor_Budapest_1956.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Szt%C3%A1lin_szobor_Budapest_1956.jpg/330px-Szt%C3%A1lin_szobor_Budapest_1956.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Szt%C3%A1lin_szobor_Budapest_1956.jpg/440px-Szt%C3%A1lin_szobor_Budapest_1956.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5300" data-file-height="3423" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Szt%C3%A1lin_szobor_Budapest_1956.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Placing of Hungarian flag into remains of dismantled Stalin statue</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At 20:00, First Secretary <a href="/wiki/Ern%C5%91_Ger%C5%91" title="Ernő Gerő">Ernő Gerő</a> broadcast a speech condemning the writers' and students' demands.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_report_2C_p55_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_report_2C_p55-48">[48]</a></sup> Angered by Gerő's hard-line rejection, some demonstrators decided to carry out one of their demands, the removal of <a href="/wiki/Stalin_Monument_in_Budapest" class="mw-redirect" title="Stalin Monument in Budapest">Stalin's 30-foot-high (9.1&#160;m) bronze statue</a> that was erected in 1951 on the site of a church, which was demolished to make room for the monument.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> By 21:30, the statue was toppled and crowds celebrated by placing <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Hungary" title="Flag of Hungary">Hungarian flags</a> in Stalin's boots, which was all that was left of the statue.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_report_2C_p55_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_report_2C_p55-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>At about the same time, a large crowd gathered at the <a href="/wiki/Radio_Budapest" class="mw-redirect" title="Radio Budapest">Radio Budapest</a> building, which was heavily guarded by the ÁVH. The flash point was reached as a delegation attempting to broadcast their demands was detained and the crowd grew increasingly unruly as rumours spread that the protesters had been shot. Tear gas was thrown from the upper windows and the ÁVH opened fire on the crowd, killing many.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_report_2C_p56_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_report_2C_p56-50">[50]</a></sup> The ÁVH tried to re-supply itself by hiding arms inside an ambulance, but the crowd detected the ruse and intercepted it. Hungarian soldiers sent to relieve the ÁVH hesitated and then, tearing the red stars from their caps, sided with the crowd.<sup id="cite_ref-Heller_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heller-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_report_2C_p56_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_report_2C_p56-50">[50]</a></sup> Provoked by the ÁVH attack, protesters reacted violently. Police cars were set ablaze, guns were seized from military depots and distributed to the masses and symbols of the Communist regime were vandalised.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fighting_spreads.2C_government_falls">Fighting spreads, government falls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fighting spreads, government falls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the night of 23 October, Hungarian Working People's Party Secretary Ernő Gerő requested Soviet military intervention "to suppress a demonstration that was reaching an ever greater and unprecedented scale."<sup id="cite_ref-satellite_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-satellite-35">[35]</a></sup> The Soviet leadership had formulated contingency plans for intervention in Hungary several months before.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> By 02:00 on 24 October, acting in accordance with orders of <a href="/wiki/Georgy_Zhukov" title="Georgy Zhukov">Georgy Zhukov</a>, the Soviet defence minister, Soviet tanks entered Budapest.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>By noon, on 24 October, Soviet tanks were stationed outside the Parliament, and Soviet soldiers guarded key bridges and crossroads. Armed revolutionaries quickly set up barricades to defend Budapest, and were reported to have already captured some Soviet tanks by mid-morning.<sup id="cite_ref-Heller_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heller-46">[46]</a></sup> That day, Imre Nagy replaced <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A1s_Heged%C3%BCs" title="András Hegedüs">András Hegedüs</a> as Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> On the radio, Nagy called for an end to violence and promised to initiate political reforms that had been shelved three years earlier. The population continued to arm itself as sporadic violence erupted.<sup id="cite_ref-Chapter_II.C.2C_para_57_p._20_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chapter_II.C.2C_para_57_p._20-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Armed protesters seized the radio building. At the offices of the Communist newspaper <i>Szabad Nép</i> unarmed demonstrators were fired upon by ÁVH guards who were then driven out as armed demonstrators arrived.<sup id="cite_ref-Chapter_II.C.2C_para_57_p._20_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chapter_II.C.2C_para_57_p._20-55">[55]</a></sup> At this point, the revolutionaries' wrath focused on the ÁVH;<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Soviet military units were not yet fully engaged, and there were reports of some Soviet troops showing open sympathy for the demonstrators.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kossuth_Lajos_utca_a_Ferenciek_tere_fel%C5%91l_n%C3%A9zve._1956._okt%C3%B3ber_25-e_d%C3%A9lut%C3%A1n,_-_Fortepan_24652.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Kossuth_Lajos_utca_a_Ferenciek_tere_fel%C5%91l_n%C3%A9zve._1956._okt%C3%B3ber_25-e_d%C3%A9lut%C3%A1n%2C_-_Fortepan_24652.jpg/220px-Kossuth_Lajos_utca_a_Ferenciek_tere_fel%C5%91l_n%C3%A9zve._1956._okt%C3%B3ber_25-e_d%C3%A9lut%C3%A1n%2C_-_Fortepan_24652.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Kossuth_Lajos_utca_a_Ferenciek_tere_fel%C5%91l_n%C3%A9zve._1956._okt%C3%B3ber_25-e_d%C3%A9lut%C3%A1n%2C_-_Fortepan_24652.jpg/330px-Kossuth_Lajos_utca_a_Ferenciek_tere_fel%C5%91l_n%C3%A9zve._1956._okt%C3%B3ber_25-e_d%C3%A9lut%C3%A1n%2C_-_Fortepan_24652.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Kossuth_Lajos_utca_a_Ferenciek_tere_fel%C5%91l_n%C3%A9zve._1956._okt%C3%B3ber_25-e_d%C3%A9lut%C3%A1n%2C_-_Fortepan_24652.jpg/440px-Kossuth_Lajos_utca_a_Ferenciek_tere_fel%C5%91l_n%C3%A9zve._1956._okt%C3%B3ber_25-e_d%C3%A9lut%C3%A1n%2C_-_Fortepan_24652.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4840" data-file-height="3103" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kossuth_Lajos_utca_a_Ferenciek_tere_fel%C5%91l_n%C3%A9zve._1956._okt%C3%B3ber_25-e_d%C3%A9lut%C3%A1n,_-_Fortepan_24652.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
March of protesters on 25th October</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 25 October, a mass of protesters gathered in front of the Parliament Building. ÁVH units began shooting into the crowd from the rooftops of neighbouring buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-Chapter_X.I.2C_para_482_p._153_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chapter_X.I.2C_para_482_p._153-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Some Soviet soldiers returned fire on the ÁVH, mistakenly believing that they were the targets of the shooting.<sup id="cite_ref-Heller_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heller-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Supplied by arms taken from the ÁVH or given by Hungarian soldiers who joined the uprising, some in the crowd started shooting back.<sup id="cite_ref-Heller_46-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heller-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chapter_X.I.2C_para_482_p._153_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chapter_X.I.2C_para_482_p._153-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>During this time, the Hungarian Army was divided as the central command structure disintegrated with the rising pressures from the protests on the government. The majority of Hungarian military units in Budapest and the countryside remained uninvolved, as the local commanders generally avoided using force against the protesters and revolutionaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Lendvai_2008_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lendvai_2008-61">[61]</a></sup> From 24 to 29 October, however, there were 71 cases of armed clashes between the army and the populace in fifty communities, ranging from the defence of attacks on civilian and military objectives to fighting with insurgents depending on the commanding officer.<sup id="cite_ref-Lendvai_2008_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lendvai_2008-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>One example is in the town of Kecskemét on 26 October, where demonstrations in front of the office of State Security and the local jail led to military action by the Third Corps under the orders of Major General Lajos Gyurkó, in which seven protesters were shot and several of the organizers were arrested. In another case, a fighter jet strafed a protest in the town of <a href="/wiki/Tiszak%C3%A9cske" title="Tiszakécske">Tiszakécske</a>, killing 17 people and wounding 117.<sup id="cite_ref-Lendvai_2008_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lendvai_2008-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>The attacks at the Parliament forced the collapse of the government.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Communist First Secretary Ernő Gerő and former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A1s_Heged%C3%BCs" title="András Hegedüs">András Hegedüs</a> fled to the Soviet Union; <a href="/wiki/Imre_Nagy" title="Imre Nagy">Imre Nagy</a> became Prime Minister and <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1nos_K%C3%A1d%C3%A1r" title="János Kádár">János Kádár</a> First Secretary of the Communist Party.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Revolutionaries began an aggressive offensive against Soviet troops and the remnants of the ÁVH.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:II._J%C3%A1nos_P%C3%A1l_p%C3%A1pa_(K%C3%B6zt%C3%A1rsas%C3%A1g)_t%C3%A9r,_a_p%C3%A1rth%C3%A1z_ostromakor_kiv%C3%A9gzett_v%C3%A9d%C5%91_holtteste._Fortepan_24462.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/II._J%C3%A1nos_P%C3%A1l_p%C3%A1pa_%28K%C3%B6zt%C3%A1rsas%C3%A1g%29_t%C3%A9r%2C_a_p%C3%A1rth%C3%A1z_ostromakor_kiv%C3%A9gzett_v%C3%A9d%C5%91_holtteste._Fortepan_24462.jpg/220px-II._J%C3%A1nos_P%C3%A1l_p%C3%A1pa_%28K%C3%B6zt%C3%A1rsas%C3%A1g%29_t%C3%A9r%2C_a_p%C3%A1rth%C3%A1z_ostromakor_kiv%C3%A9gzett_v%C3%A9d%C5%91_holtteste._Fortepan_24462.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/II._J%C3%A1nos_P%C3%A1l_p%C3%A1pa_%28K%C3%B6zt%C3%A1rsas%C3%A1g%29_t%C3%A9r%2C_a_p%C3%A1rth%C3%A1z_ostromakor_kiv%C3%A9gzett_v%C3%A9d%C5%91_holtteste._Fortepan_24462.jpg/330px-II._J%C3%A1nos_P%C3%A1l_p%C3%A1pa_%28K%C3%B6zt%C3%A1rsas%C3%A1g%29_t%C3%A9r%2C_a_p%C3%A1rth%C3%A1z_ostromakor_kiv%C3%A9gzett_v%C3%A9d%C5%91_holtteste._Fortepan_24462.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/II._J%C3%A1nos_P%C3%A1l_p%C3%A1pa_%28K%C3%B6zt%C3%A1rsas%C3%A1g%29_t%C3%A9r%2C_a_p%C3%A1rth%C3%A1z_ostromakor_kiv%C3%A9gzett_v%C3%A9d%C5%91_holtteste._Fortepan_24462.jpg/440px-II._J%C3%A1nos_P%C3%A1l_p%C3%A1pa_%28K%C3%B6zt%C3%A1rsas%C3%A1g%29_t%C3%A9r%2C_a_p%C3%A1rth%C3%A1z_ostromakor_kiv%C3%A9gzett_v%C3%A9d%C5%91_holtteste._Fortepan_24462.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4674" data-file-height="2974" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:II._J%C3%A1nos_P%C3%A1l_p%C3%A1pa_(K%C3%B6zt%C3%A1rsas%C3%A1g)_t%C3%A9r,_a_p%C3%A1rth%C3%A1z_ostromakor_kiv%C3%A9gzett_v%C3%A9d%C5%91_holtteste._Fortepan_24462.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Body of executed Party member at Central Committee of the Communist Party</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Units led by <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9la_Kir%C3%A1ly" title="Béla Király">Béla Király</a>, after attacking the building of the Central Committee of the Communist Party, executed dozens of suspected communists, state security members, and military personnel. Photographs showed victims with signs of torture. On 30 October, Király's forces attacked the Central Committee of the Communist Party building.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Hungarian politician János Berecz referred to how rebels detained thousands of people, and that thousands more had their names on death lists. In the city of <a href="/wiki/Kaposv%C3%A1r" title="Kaposvár">Kaposvár</a>, 64 persons including 13 army officers were detained on 31 October.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Budapest and other areas, the Hungarian Communist committees organised defence. At the <a href="/wiki/Csepel" title="Csepel">Csepel</a> neighbourhood of Budapest, some 250 Communists defended the Csepel Iron and Steel Works. On 27 October, army units were brought in to secure Csepel and restore order. They later withdrew on 29 October, after which the rebels seized control of the area. Communists of Budapest neighbourhood <a href="/wiki/Angyalf%C3%B6ld" title="Angyalföld">Angyalföld</a> led more than 350 armed workers and 380 servicemen from the Láng Factory. Anti-fascist resistance veterans from World War II participated in the offensive by which the <i>Szabad Nép</i> newspaper's building was recaptured. In the countryside, defence measures were taken by pro-Communist forces. In <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9k%C3%A9s_County" title="Békés County">Békés County</a>, in and around the town of <a href="/wiki/Szarvas" title="Szarvas">Szarvas</a>, the armed guards of the Communist Party were in control throughout.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the Hungarian resistance fought Soviet tanks using <a href="/wiki/Molotov_cocktail" title="Molotov cocktail">Molotov cocktails</a> in the narrow streets of Budapest, <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_council" title="Workers' council">revolutionary councils</a> arose nationwide, assumed local governmental authority, and called for general strikes. Public Communist symbols such as <a href="/wiki/Red_star" title="Red star">red stars</a> and Soviet war memorials were removed, and Communist books were burned. Spontaneous revolutionary militias arose, such as the 400-man group loosely led by <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zsef_Dud%C3%A1s" title="József Dudás">József Dudás</a>, which attacked or murdered Soviet sympathisers and ÁVH members.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Soviet units fought primarily in Budapest; elsewhere the countryside was largely quiet. One armoured division stationed in Budapest, commanded by <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A1l_Mal%C3%A9ter" title="Pál Maléter">Pál Maléter</a>, instead opted to join the insurgents. Soviet commanders often negotiated local cease-fires with the revolutionaries.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>In some regions, Soviet forces managed to quell revolutionary activity. In Budapest, the Soviets were eventually fought to a stand-still and hostilities began to wane. Hungarian general <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9la_Kir%C3%A1ly" title="Béla Király">Béla Király</a>, freed from a life sentence for political offences and acting with the support of the Nagy government, sought to restore order by unifying elements of the police, army and insurgent groups into a National Guard.<sup id="cite_ref-ng_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ng-69">[69]</a></sup> A ceasefire was arranged on 28 October, and by 30 October most Soviet troops had withdrawn from Budapest to garrisons in the Hungarian countryside.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interlude">Interlude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Interlude">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fighting ceased between 28 October and 4 November, as many Hungarians believed that Soviet military units were withdrawing from Hungary.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> There were approximately 213 Hungarian Working People's Party members lynched or executed during this period.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Government">New Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: New Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flyer._Imre_Nagy,_Head_of_goverment_-1956.10.27.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Flyer._Imre_Nagy%2C_Head_of_goverment_-1956.10.27.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Flyer._Imre_Nagy%2C_Head_of_goverment_-1956.10.27.tif.jpg" width="220" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Flyer._Imre_Nagy%2C_Head_of_goverment_-1956.10.27.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Flyer._Imre_Nagy%2C_Head_of_goverment_-1956.10.27.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Flyer._Imre_Nagy%2C_Head_of_goverment_-1956.10.27.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Flyer._Imre_Nagy%2C_Head_of_goverment_-1956.10.27.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4172" data-file-height="5393" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flyer._Imre_Nagy,_Head_of_goverment_-1956.10.27.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Flyer. Imre Nagy, Head of government – 1956.10.27</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The rapid spread of the uprising in the streets of Budapest and the abrupt fall of the Gerő-Hegedüs government left the new national leadership surprised, and at first disorganised. Nagy, a loyal party reformer described as possessing "only modest political skills",<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> initially appealed to the public for calm and a return to the old order. Yet Nagy, the only remaining Hungarian leader with credibility in both the eyes of the public and the Soviets, "at long last concluded that a popular uprising rather than a counter-revolution was taking place".<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> At 13:20 on 28 October, Nagy announced an immediate and general cease-fire over the radio and, on behalf of the new national government, declared the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>that the government would assess the uprising not as counter-revolutionary but as a "great, national and democratic event"</li>
<li>an unconditional general ceasefire and amnesty for those who participated in the uprising; negotiations with the insurgents</li>
<li>the dissolution of the ÁVH</li>
<li>the establishment of a national guard</li>
<li>the immediate withdrawal of Soviet troops from Budapest and negotiations for the withdrawal of all Soviet forces from Hungary</li>
</ul>
<p>On 1 November, in a radio address to the Hungarian people, Nagy formally declared Hungary's withdrawal from the Warsaw Pact as well as Hungary's stance of neutrality.<sup id="cite_ref-Lendvai_2008_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lendvai_2008-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Because it held office only ten days, the National Government had little chance to clarify its policies in detail. However, newspaper editorials at the time stressed that Hungary should be a neutral, multi-party <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social democracy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Many political prisoners were released, most notably <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_J%C3%B3zsef_Mindszenty" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardinal József Mindszenty">Cardinal József Mindszenty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Political parties that were previously banned, such as the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Smallholders,_Agrarian_Workers_and_Civic_Party" title="Independent Smallholders, Agrarian Workers and Civic Party">Independent Smallholders</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Peasant_Party_(Hungary)" title="National Peasant Party (Hungary)">National Peasant Party</a> (under the name "Petőfi Party"),<sup id="cite_ref-VM_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VM-79">[79]</a></sup> reappeared to join the coalition.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kossuth_Lajos_utca_-_K%C3%A1roly_(Tan%C3%A1cs)_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_sarok._Fortepan_23591.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Kossuth_Lajos_utca_-_K%C3%A1roly_%28Tan%C3%A1cs%29_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_sarok._Fortepan_23591.jpg/220px-Kossuth_Lajos_utca_-_K%C3%A1roly_%28Tan%C3%A1cs%29_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_sarok._Fortepan_23591.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Kossuth_Lajos_utca_-_K%C3%A1roly_%28Tan%C3%A1cs%29_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_sarok._Fortepan_23591.jpg/330px-Kossuth_Lajos_utca_-_K%C3%A1roly_%28Tan%C3%A1cs%29_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_sarok._Fortepan_23591.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Kossuth_Lajos_utca_-_K%C3%A1roly_%28Tan%C3%A1cs%29_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_sarok._Fortepan_23591.jpg/440px-Kossuth_Lajos_utca_-_K%C3%A1roly_%28Tan%C3%A1cs%29_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_sarok._Fortepan_23591.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5419" data-file-height="3576" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kossuth_Lajos_utca_-_K%C3%A1roly_(Tan%C3%A1cs)_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_sarok._Fortepan_23591.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Crowd cheers Hungarian troops in Budapest</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During this time, in 1,170 communities across Hungary there were 348 cases in which revolutionary councils and protesters dismissed employees of the local administrative councils, 312 cases in which they sacked the persons in charge, and 215 cases in which they burned the local administrative files and records. In addition, in 681 communities demonstrators damaged symbols of Soviet authority such as red stars, Stalin or Lenin statues; 393 in which they damaged Soviet war memorials, and 122 communities in which book burnings took place.<sup id="cite_ref-Litv.C3.A1n_1996_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Litv.C3.A1n_1996-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lendvai_2008_61-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lendvai_2008-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Local <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_council" title="Workers' council">revolutionary councils</a> formed throughout Hungary,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> generally without involvement from the preoccupied National Government in Budapest, and assumed various responsibilities of local government from the defunct Communist party.<sup id="cite_ref-rc_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rc-85">[85]</a></sup> By 30 October, these councils had been officially sanctioned by the Hungarian Working People's Party, and the Nagy government asked for their support as "autonomous, democratic local organs formed during the Revolution".<sup id="cite_ref-rc_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rc-85">[85]</a></sup> Likewise, <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_councils" class="mw-redirect" title="Workers' councils">workers' councils</a> were established at industrial plants and mines, and many unpopular regulations such as production norms were eliminated. The workers' councils strove to manage the enterprise while protecting workers' interests, thus establishing a socialist economy free of rigid party control.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Local control by the councils was not always bloodless; in <a href="/wiki/Debrecen" title="Debrecen">Debrecen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gy%C5%91r" title="Győr">Győr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sopron" title="Sopron">Sopron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mosonmagyar%C3%B3v%C3%A1r" title="Mosonmagyaróvár">Mosonmagyaróvár</a> and other cities, crowds of demonstrators were fired upon by the ÁVH, with many lives lost. The ÁVH were disarmed, often by force, in many cases assisted by the local police.<sup id="cite_ref-rc_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rc-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>In total there were approximately 2,100 local revolutionary and workers councils with over 28,000 members. These councils held a combined conference in Budapest decided to end the nationwide labour strikes and resume work on 5 November, with the more important councils sending delegates to the Parliament to assure the Nagy government of their support.<sup id="cite_ref-Lendvai_2008_61-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lendvai_2008-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Soviet_perspective">Soviet perspective</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Soviet perspective">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 24 October, the <a href="/wiki/Politburo_of_the_Central_Committee_of_the_Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Politburo of the Central Committee of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union">Presidium of the Central Committee of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union</a> (the Politburo) discussed the political upheavals <a href="/wiki/Polish_October" title="Polish October">in Poland</a> and Hungary. A hard-line faction led by <a href="/wiki/Vyacheslav_Molotov" title="Vyacheslav Molotov">Molotov</a> was pushing for intervention, but Khrushchev and Marshal <a href="/wiki/Georgy_Zhukov" title="Georgy Zhukov">Zhukov</a> were initially opposed. A delegation in Budapest reported that the situation was not as dire as had been portrayed. Khrushchev stated that he believed that Party Secretary Ernő Gerő's request for intervention on 23 October indicated that the Hungarian Party still held the confidence of the Hungarian public. In addition, he saw the protests not as an ideological struggle, but as popular discontent over unresolved basic economic and social issues.<sup id="cite_ref-satellite_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-satellite-35">[35]</a></sup> The concurrent <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a> was another reason to not intervene; as Khrushchev said on 28 October, it would be a mistake to imitate the "real mess" of the French and British.<sup id="cite_ref-mastny200203_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mastny200203-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>After some debate,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> the Presidium on 30 October decided not to remove the new Hungarian government. Even Marshal <a href="/wiki/Georgy_Zhukov" title="Georgy Zhukov">Georgy Zhukov</a> said: "We should withdraw troops from Budapest, and if necessary withdraw from Hungary as a whole. This is a lesson for us in the military-political sphere." They adopted a <i>Declaration of the Government of the USSR on the Principles of Development and Further Strengthening of Friendship and Cooperation between the Soviet Union and other Socialist States</i>, which was issued the next day. This document proclaimed: "The Soviet Government is prepared to enter into the appropriate negotiations with the government of the Hungarian People's Republic and other members of the Warsaw Treaty on the question of the presence of Soviet troops on the territory of Hungary."<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Thus for a brief moment it looked like there could be a peaceful solution.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:II._J%C3%A1nos_P%C3%A1l_p%C3%A1pa_(K%C3%B6zt%C3%A1rsas%C3%A1g)_t%C3%A9r,_MDP_Budapesti_P%C3%A1rtbizotts%C3%A1g%C3%A1nak_sz%C3%A9kh%C3%A1za._Fortepan_24495.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/II._J%C3%A1nos_P%C3%A1l_p%C3%A1pa_%28K%C3%B6zt%C3%A1rsas%C3%A1g%29_t%C3%A9r%2C_MDP_Budapesti_P%C3%A1rtbizotts%C3%A1g%C3%A1nak_sz%C3%A9kh%C3%A1za._Fortepan_24495.jpg/220px-II._J%C3%A1nos_P%C3%A1l_p%C3%A1pa_%28K%C3%B6zt%C3%A1rsas%C3%A1g%29_t%C3%A9r%2C_MDP_Budapesti_P%C3%A1rtbizotts%C3%A1g%C3%A1nak_sz%C3%A9kh%C3%A1za._Fortepan_24495.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/II._J%C3%A1nos_P%C3%A1l_p%C3%A1pa_%28K%C3%B6zt%C3%A1rsas%C3%A1g%29_t%C3%A9r%2C_MDP_Budapesti_P%C3%A1rtbizotts%C3%A1g%C3%A1nak_sz%C3%A9kh%C3%A1za._Fortepan_24495.jpg/330px-II._J%C3%A1nos_P%C3%A1l_p%C3%A1pa_%28K%C3%B6zt%C3%A1rsas%C3%A1g%29_t%C3%A9r%2C_MDP_Budapesti_P%C3%A1rtbizotts%C3%A1g%C3%A1nak_sz%C3%A9kh%C3%A1za._Fortepan_24495.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/II._J%C3%A1nos_P%C3%A1l_p%C3%A1pa_%28K%C3%B6zt%C3%A1rsas%C3%A1g%29_t%C3%A9r%2C_MDP_Budapesti_P%C3%A1rtbizotts%C3%A1g%C3%A1nak_sz%C3%A9kh%C3%A1za._Fortepan_24495.jpg/440px-II._J%C3%A1nos_P%C3%A1l_p%C3%A1pa_%28K%C3%B6zt%C3%A1rsas%C3%A1g%29_t%C3%A9r%2C_MDP_Budapesti_P%C3%A1rtbizotts%C3%A1g%C3%A1nak_sz%C3%A9kh%C3%A1za._Fortepan_24495.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4768" data-file-height="3027" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:II._J%C3%A1nos_P%C3%A1l_p%C3%A1pa_(K%C3%B6zt%C3%A1rsas%C3%A1g)_t%C3%A9r,_MDP_Budapesti_P%C3%A1rtbizotts%C3%A1g%C3%A1nak_sz%C3%A9kh%C3%A1za._Fortepan_24495.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Damaged Party headquarters on Köztársaság tér</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 30 October, armed protesters attacked the ÁVH detachment guarding the Budapest Hungarian Working People's Party headquarters on Köztársaság tér (Republic Square), incited by rumours of prisoners held there and the earlier shootings of demonstrators by the ÁVH in the city of <a href="/wiki/Mosonmagyar%C3%B3v%C3%A1r" title="Mosonmagyaróvár">Mosonmagyaróvár</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rc_85-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rc-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kramer1_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kramer1-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-revhu_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-revhu-92">[92]</a></sup> Over 20 ÁVH officers were killed, some of them lynched by the mob. Hungarian army tanks sent to rescue the party headquarters mistakenly bombarded the building.<sup id="cite_ref-revhu_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-revhu-92">[92]</a></sup> The head of the Budapest party committee, Imre Mező, was wounded and later died.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-parsons_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parsons-94">[94]</a></sup> Scenes from Republic Square were shown on Soviet newsreels a few hours later.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Revolutionary leaders in Hungary condemned the incident and appealed for calm, and the mob violence soon died down,<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> but images of the victims were nevertheless used as <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a> by various Communist organs.<sup id="cite_ref-parsons_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parsons-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 31 October the Soviet leaders decided to reverse their decision from the previous day. There is disagreement among historians whether Hungary's declaration to exit the Warsaw Pact caused the second Soviet intervention. Minutes of the 31 October meeting of the Presidium record that the decision to intervene militarily was taken one day before Hungary declared its neutrality and withdrawal from the Warsaw Pact.<sup id="cite_ref-presidium_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presidium-97">[97]</a></sup> Historians who deny that Hungarian neutrality—or other factors such as Western inaction in Hungary or perceived Western weakness due to the Suez crisis—caused the intervention state that the Soviet decision was based solely on the rapid loss of Communist control in Hungary.<sup id="cite_ref-mastny200203_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mastny200203-87">[87]</a></sup> However, some Russian historians who are not advocates of the Communist era maintain that the Hungarian declaration of neutrality caused the Kremlin to intervene a second time.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two days earlier, on 30 October, when Soviet Politburo representatives <a href="/wiki/Anastas_Mikoyan" title="Anastas Mikoyan">Anastas Mikoyan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Suslov" title="Mikhail Suslov">Mikhail Suslov</a> were in Budapest, Nagy had hinted that neutrality was a long-term objective for Hungary, and that he was hoping to discuss this matter with the leaders in the Kremlin. This information was passed on to Moscow by Mikoyan and Suslov.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> At that time, Khrushchev was in Stalin's <a href="/wiki/Dacha" title="Dacha">dacha</a>, considering his options regarding Hungary. One of his speech writers later said that the declaration of neutrality was an important factor in his subsequent decision to support intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> In addition, some Hungarian leaders of the revolution as well as students had called for their country's withdrawal from the Warsaw Pact much earlier, and this may have influenced Soviet decision making.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several other key events alarmed the Presidium and cemented the interventionists' position:<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-presidium1nov_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presidium1nov-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Simultaneous movements towards multi-party parliamentary democracy, and a democratic national council of workers, which could "lead towards a capitalist state." Both movements challenged the pre-eminence of the Soviet Communist Party in Eastern Europe and perhaps Soviet <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">hegemony</a> itself. For the majority of the Presidium, the workers' direct control over their councils without Communist Party leadership was incompatible with their idea of socialism. At the time, these councils were, in the words of <a href="/wiki/Hannah_Arendt" title="Hannah Arendt">Hannah Arendt</a>, "the only free and acting <a href="/wiki/Soviet_(council)" title="Soviet (council)">soviets (councils)</a> in existence anywhere in the world".<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li>Khrushchev stated that many in the Communist Party would not understand a failure to respond with force in Hungary. <a href="/wiki/Destalinisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Destalinisation">Destalinisation</a> had alienated the more conservative elements of the Party, who were alarmed at threats to Soviet influence in Eastern Europe. On 17 June 1953, workers in <a href="/wiki/East_Berlin" title="East Berlin">East Berlin</a> had staged an <a href="/wiki/Uprising_of_1953_in_East_Germany" title="Uprising of 1953 in East Germany">uprising</a>, demanding the resignation of the government of the <a href="/wiki/German_Democratic_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="German Democratic Republic">German Democratic Republic</a>. This was quickly and violently put down with the help of the Soviet military, with 84 killed and wounded and 700 arrested.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> In June 1956, in <a href="/wiki/Pozna%C5%84" title="Poznań">Poznań</a>, Poland, an anti-government <a href="/wiki/Pozna%C5%84_1956_protests" title="Poznań 1956 protests">workers' revolt</a> had been suppressed by the Polish security forces with between 57<sup id="cite_ref-Paczkowski_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paczkowski-108">[108]</a></sup> and 78<sup id="cite_ref-Jastrz.C4.85b_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jastrz.C4.85b-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-W.C3.B3jtowocz_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W.C3.B3jtowocz-110">[110]</a></sup> deaths and led to <a href="/wiki/Polish_October" title="Polish October">the installation of a less Soviet-controlled government</a>. Additionally, by late October, unrest was noticed in some regional areas of the Soviet Union: while this unrest was minor, it was intolerable.</li>
<li>Hungarian neutrality and withdrawal from the Warsaw Pact represented a breach in the Soviet defensive <a href="/wiki/Buffer_zone" title="Buffer zone">buffer zone</a> of <a href="/wiki/Satellite_nation" class="mw-redirect" title="Satellite nation">satellite nations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Soviet fear of invasion from the West made a defensive buffer of allied states in Eastern Europe an essential security objective.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Szent_Istv%C3%A1n_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_a_Falk_Miksa_(N%C3%A9phadsereg)_utca_fel%C5%91l_a_Honv%C3%A9d_utca_fel%C3%A9_n%C3%A9zve._A_szovjet_csapatok_ideiglenes_kivonul%C3%A1sa_1956._okt%C3%B3ber_31-%C3%A9n._Fortepan_24787.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Szent_Istv%C3%A1n_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_a_Falk_Miksa_%28N%C3%A9phadsereg%29_utca_fel%C5%91l_a_Honv%C3%A9d_utca_fel%C3%A9_n%C3%A9zve._A_szovjet_csapatok_ideiglenes_kivonul%C3%A1sa_1956._okt%C3%B3ber_31-%C3%A9n._Fortepan_24787.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Szent_Istv%C3%A1n_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_a_Falk_Miksa_%28N%C3%A9phadsereg%29_utca_fel%C5%91l_a_Honv%C3%A9d_utca_fel%C3%A9_n%C3%A9zve._A_szovjet_csapatok_ideiglenes_kivonul%C3%A1sa_1956._okt%C3%B3ber_31-%C3%A9n._Fortepan_24787.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Szent_Istv%C3%A1n_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_a_Falk_Miksa_%28N%C3%A9phadsereg%29_utca_fel%C5%91l_a_Honv%C3%A9d_utca_fel%C3%A9_n%C3%A9zve._A_szovjet_csapatok_ideiglenes_kivonul%C3%A1sa_1956._okt%C3%B3ber_31-%C3%A9n._Fortepan_24787.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4570" data-file-height="3146" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Szent_Istv%C3%A1n_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_a_Falk_Miksa_(N%C3%A9phadsereg)_utca_fel%C5%91l_a_Honv%C3%A9d_utca_fel%C3%A9_n%C3%A9zve._A_szovjet_csapatok_ideiglenes_kivonul%C3%A1sa_1956._okt%C3%B3ber_31-%C3%A9n._Fortepan_24787.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Soviet <a href="/wiki/T-54" class="mw-redirect" title="T-54">T-54</a> tanks in Budapest on 31st October</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the light of what was taking place in China and the news from Budapest, these militants arrived at the conclusion that "the Party is the incarnation of bureaucratic despotism" and that "socialism can develop only on the foundations of direct democracy". For them the struggle of the Hungarian workers was a struggle "for the principle of direct democracy" and "all power should be transferred to the Workers Committees of Hungary".<sup id="cite_ref-James2013_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James2013-112">[112]</a></sup> The Presidium decided to break the <i>de facto</i> ceasefire and crush the Hungarian revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> The plan was to declare a "Provisional Revolutionary Government" under János Kádár, who would appeal for Soviet assistance to restore order. According to witnesses, Kádár was in Moscow in early November,<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> and he was in contact with the Soviet embassy while still a member of the Nagy government.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Delegations were sent to other Communist governments in Eastern Europe and China, seeking to avoid a regional conflict, and <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a> messages prepared for broadcast when the second Soviet intervention had begun. To disguise these intentions, Soviet diplomats were to engage the Nagy government in talks discussing the withdrawal of Soviet forces.<sup id="cite_ref-presidium_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presidium-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to some sources, the Chinese leader <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a> played an important role in Khrushchev's decision to suppress the Hungarian uprising. Chinese Communist Party Deputy Chairman <a href="/wiki/Liu_Shaoqi" title="Liu Shaoqi">Liu Shaoqi</a> pressured Khrushchev to send in troops to put down the revolt by force.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Although the relations between China and the Soviet Union had deteriorated during the recent years, Mao's words still carried great weight in the Kremlin, and they were frequently in contact during the crisis. Initially, Mao opposed a second intervention, and this information was passed on to Khrushchev on 30 October, before the Presidium met and decided against intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Mao then changed his mind in favour of intervention but, according to <a href="/wiki/William_Taubman" title="William Taubman">William Taubman</a>, it remains unclear when and how Khrushchev learned of this and thus if it influenced his decision on 31 October.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 1 to 3 November, Khrushchev left Moscow to meet with his European allies and inform them of the decision to intervene. At the first such meeting, he met with <a href="/wiki/W%C5%82adys%C5%82aw_Gomu%C5%82ka" title="Władysław Gomułka">Władysław Gomułka</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brest,_Belarus" title="Brest, Belarus">Brest</a>. Then, he had talks with the Romanian, Czechoslovak, and Bulgarian leaders in <a href="/wiki/Bucharest" title="Bucharest">Bucharest</a>. Finally Khrushchev flew with <a href="/wiki/Malenkov" class="mw-redirect" title="Malenkov">Malenkov</a> to Yugoslavia, where they met with <a href="/wiki/Josip_Broz_Tito" title="Josip Broz Tito">Josip Broz Tito</a>, who was on holiday on his island <a href="/wiki/Brijuni" title="Brijuni">Brioni</a> in the Adriatic.
