The Yugoslavs also persuaded Khrushchev to choose <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1nos_K%C3%A1d%C3%A1r" title="János Kádár">János Kádár</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Ferenc_M%C3%BCnnich" title="Ferenc Münnich">Ferenc Münnich</a> as the new leader of Hungary.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Two months after the Soviet crackdown, Tito confided in Nikolai Firiubin, the Soviet ambassador to Yugoslavia, that "the reaction raised its head, especially in Croatia, where the reactionary elements openly incited the employees of the Yugoslav security organs to violence."<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="International_reaction">International reaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: International reaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Although <a href="/wiki/John_Foster_Dulles" title="John Foster Dulles">John Foster Dulles</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">United States Secretary of State</a> recommended on 24 October for the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a> to convene to discuss the situation in Hungary, little immediate action was taken to introduce a resolution,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> in part because other world events unfolded the day after the peaceful interlude started, when <a href="/wiki/Protocol_of_S%C3%A8vres" title="Protocol of Sèvres">allied collusion</a> started the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a>. The problem was not that Suez distracted US attention from Hungary but that it made the condemnation of Soviet actions very difficult. As Vice President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> later explained, "We couldn't on one hand, complain about the Soviets intervening in Hungary and, on the other hand, approve of the British and the French picking that particular time to intervene against [Gamel Abdel] <a href="/wiki/Nasser" class="mw-redirect" title="Nasser">Nasser</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-BorhiContRoll_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BorhiContRoll-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>The US response was reliant on the CIA to covertly effect change, with both covert agents and <a href="/wiki/Radio_Free_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Radio Free Europe">Radio Free Europe</a>. However, their Hungarian operations collapsed rapidly and they could not locate any of the weapon caches hidden across Europe, nor be sure who they'd send arms too. The agency's main source of information were the newspapers and a State Department employee in Budapest called Geza Katona.<sup id="cite_ref-Ashes_page_149_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashes_page_149-39">[39]</a></sup> By the 28th of October, on the same night that the new Nagy government came to power, RFE was ramping up its broadcasts – encouraging armed struggle, advising on how to combat tanks and signing off with "Freedom or Death!" – on the orders of <a href="/wiki/Frank_Wisner" title="Frank Wisner">Frank Wisner</a>. When Nagy did come to power, CIA director Allen Dulles advised the White House that <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zsef_Mindszenty" title="József Mindszenty">Cardinal Mindszenty</a> would be a better leader (due to Nagy's communist past); he had CIA radio broadcasts run propaganda against Nagy, calling him a traitor who'd invited Soviet troops in. Broadcasts continued to broadcast armed response while the CIA mistakenly believed that the Hungarian army was switching sides and the rebels were gaining arms.<sup id="cite_ref-Ashes_page_150_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashes_page_150-124">[124]</a></sup> (Wisner was recorded as having a "nervous breakdown" by <a href="/wiki/William_Colby" title="William Colby">William Colby</a> as the uprising was crushed<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>)</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1956_november_5._Eindhoven.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/1956_november_5._Eindhoven.jpg/220px-1956_november_5._Eindhoven.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/1956_november_5._Eindhoven.jpg/330px-1956_november_5._Eindhoven.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/1956_november_5._Eindhoven.jpg/440px-1956_november_5._Eindhoven.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1956" data-file-height="1386" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1956_november_5._Eindhoven.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
March to support Hungary in Eindhoven, 5th November 1956</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Responding to the plea by Nagy at the time of the second massive Soviet intervention on 4 November, the Security Council resolution critical of Soviet actions was vetoed by the Soviet Union; instead <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_120" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 120">resolution 120 was adopted</a> to pass the matter onto the General Assembly. The General Assembly, by a vote of 50 in favour, 8 against and 15 abstentions, called on the Soviet Union to end its Hungarian intervention, but the newly constituted Kádár government rejected UN observers.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>US President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> was aware of a detailed study of Hungarian resistance that recommended against US military intervention,<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> and of earlier policy discussions within the National Security Council that focused upon encouraging discontent in Soviet satellite nations only by economic policies and political rhetoric.<sup id="cite_ref-BorhiContRoll_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BorhiContRoll-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> In a 1998 interview, Hungarian Ambassador <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9za_Jeszenszky" title="Géza Jeszenszky">Géza Jeszenszky</a> was critical of Western inaction in 1956, citing the influence of the United Nations at that time and giving the example of <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">UN intervention in Korea</a> from 1950 to 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the uprising, the <a href="/wiki/Radio_Free_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Radio Free Europe">Radio Free Europe</a> (RFE) Hungarian-language programs broadcast news of the political and military situation, as well as appealing to Hungarians to fight the Soviet forces, including tactical advice on resistance methods. After the Soviet suppression of the revolution, RFE was criticised for having misled the Hungarian people that <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> or United Nations would intervene if citizens continued to resist.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Allen Dulles lied to Eisenhower that RFE had not promised US aid; Eisenhower believed him, as the transcripts of the broadcasts were kept secret.<sup id="cite_ref-Ashes_page_150_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashes_page_150-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soviet_intervention_of_4_November">Soviet intervention of 4 November</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Soviet intervention of 4 November">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:400px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_1&quot; poster=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/1956-11-01_War_in_Egypt.ogv/400px--1956-11-01_War_in_Egypt.ogv.jpg&quot; <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1956-11-01_War_in_Egypt.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1 November newsreel about the situation in Hungary</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 1 November, Imre Nagy received reports that Soviet forces had entered Hungary from the east and were moving towards Budapest.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Nagy sought and received assurances (which proved false) from Soviet ambassador <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Andropov" title="Yuri Andropov">Yuri Andropov</a> that the Soviet Union would not invade. The Cabinet, with János Kádár in agreement, declared Hungary's neutrality, withdrew from the Warsaw Pact, and requested assistance from the diplomatic corps in Budapest and the <a href="/wiki/Dag_Hammarskj%C3%B6ld" title="Dag Hammarskjöld">UN Secretary-General</a> to defend Hungary's neutrality.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Ambassador Andropov was asked to inform his government that Hungary would begin negotiations on the removal of Soviet forces immediately.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 3 November, a Hungarian delegation led by the Minister of Defense <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A1l_Mal%C3%A9ter" title="Pál Maléter">Pál Maléter</a> were invited to attend negotiations on Soviet withdrawal at the Soviet Military Command at <a href="/wiki/Pest_(county)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pest (county)">Tököl</a>, near Budapest. At around midnight that evening, General <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Serov" title="Ivan Serov">Ivan Serov</a>, Chief of the Soviet Security Police (<a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a>) ordered the arrest of the Hungarian delegation,<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> and the next day, the Soviet army again attacked Budapest.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fortepan_12830_R%C3%A1k%C3%B3czi_%C3%BAt_-_Ak%C3%A1cfa_utca_sarok._Ki%C3%A9gett_szovjet_BTR-152_p%C3%A1nc%C3%A9lozott_l%C3%B6v%C3%A9szsz%C3%A1ll%C3%ADt%C3%B3_j%C3%A1rm%C5%B1..jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Fortepan_12830_R%C3%A1k%C3%B3czi_%C3%BAt_-_Ak%C3%A1cfa_utca_sarok._Ki%C3%A9gett_szovjet_BTR-152_p%C3%A1nc%C3%A9lozott_l%C3%B6v%C3%A9szsz%C3%A1ll%C3%ADt%C3%B3_j%C3%A1rm%C5%B1..jpg/220px-Fortepan_12830_R%C3%A1k%C3%B3czi_%C3%BAt_-_Ak%C3%A1cfa_utca_sarok._Ki%C3%A9gett_szovjet_BTR-152_p%C3%A1nc%C3%A9lozott_l%C3%B6v%C3%A9szsz%C3%A1ll%C3%ADt%C3%B3_j%C3%A1rm%C5%B1..jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Fortepan_12830_R%C3%A1k%C3%B3czi_%C3%BAt_-_Ak%C3%A1cfa_utca_sarok._Ki%C3%A9gett_szovjet_BTR-152_p%C3%A1nc%C3%A9lozott_l%C3%B6v%C3%A9szsz%C3%A1ll%C3%ADt%C3%B3_j%C3%A1rm%C5%B1..jpg/330px-Fortepan_12830_R%C3%A1k%C3%B3czi_%C3%BAt_-_Ak%C3%A1cfa_utca_sarok._Ki%C3%A9gett_szovjet_BTR-152_p%C3%A1nc%C3%A9lozott_l%C3%B6v%C3%A9szsz%C3%A1ll%C3%ADt%C3%B3_j%C3%A1rm%C5%B1..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Fortepan_12830_R%C3%A1k%C3%B3czi_%C3%BAt_-_Ak%C3%A1cfa_utca_sarok._Ki%C3%A9gett_szovjet_BTR-152_p%C3%A1nc%C3%A9lozott_l%C3%B6v%C3%A9szsz%C3%A1ll%C3%ADt%C3%B3_j%C3%A1rm%C5%B1..jpg/440px-Fortepan_12830_R%C3%A1k%C3%B3czi_%C3%BAt_-_Ak%C3%A1cfa_utca_sarok._Ki%C3%A9gett_szovjet_BTR-152_p%C3%A1nc%C3%A9lozott_l%C3%B6v%C3%A9szsz%C3%A1ll%C3%ADt%C3%B3_j%C3%A1rm%C5%B1..jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1996" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fortepan_12830_R%C3%A1k%C3%B3czi_%C3%BAt_-_Ak%C3%A1cfa_utca_sarok._Ki%C3%A9gett_szovjet_BTR-152_p%C3%A1nc%C3%A9lozott_l%C3%B6v%C3%A9szsz%C3%A1ll%C3%ADt%C3%B3_j%C3%A1rm%C5%B1..jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Soviet armored car burns on a street in Budapest in November</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the early morning hours of 4 November, <a href="/wiki/Ferenc_M%C3%BCnnich" title="Ferenc Münnich">Ferenc Münnich</a> announced on Radio Szolnok the establishment of the "<a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Workers%27-Peasants%27_Government_of_Hungary" title="Revolutionary Workers'-Peasants' Government of Hungary">Revolutionary Workers'-Peasants' Government of Hungary</a>".</p>
<p>The second Soviet intervention, codenamed "Operation Whirlwind", was launched by <a href="/wiki/Marshal_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Marshal of the Soviet Union">Marshal</a> <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Konev" title="Ivan Konev">Ivan Konev</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-presidium1nov_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presidium1nov-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-troops_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-troops-137">[137]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Southern_Group_of_Forces" title="Southern Group of Forces">five Soviet divisions stationed in Hungary</a> before 23 October were augmented to a total strength of 17 divisions.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/8th_Mechanized_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="8th Mechanized Army">8th Mechanized Army</a> under command of Lieutenant General <a href="/wiki/Hamazasp_Babadzhanian" title="Hamazasp Babadzhanian">Hamazasp Babadzhanian</a> and the 38th Army under Lieutenant General Hadzhi-Umar Mamsurovs from the nearby <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Military_District" title="Carpathian Military District">Carpathian Military District</a> were deployed to Hungary for the operation.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Some rank-and-file Soviet soldiers reportedly believed they were being sent to <a href="/wiki/East_Berlin" title="East Berlin">Berlin</a> to fight German fascists.<sup id="cite_ref-Fryer_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fryer-140">[140]</a></sup> By 21:30 on 3 November, the Soviet Army had completely encircled Budapest.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_report_5C_p196_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_report_5C_p196-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>At 03:00 on 4 November, Soviet tanks penetrated Budapest along the <a href="/wiki/Pest_(city)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pest (city)">Pest</a> side of the Danube in two thrusts: one up the Soroksári road from the south and the other down the Váci road from the north. Thus before a single shot was fired, the Soviets had effectively split the city in half, controlled all bridgeheads, and were shielded to the rear by the wide Danube river. Armoured units crossed into <a href="/wiki/Buda" title="Buda">Buda</a> and at 04:25 fired the first shots at the army barracks on Budaörsi Road. Soon after, Soviet artillery and tank fire was heard in all districts of Budapest.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_report_5C_p196_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_report_5C_p196-141">[141]</a></sup> Operation Whirlwind combined air strikes, artillery, and the co-ordinated tank-infantry action of 17 divisions.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:J%C3%B3zsef_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_a_Corvin_(Kisfaludy)_k%C3%B6zn%C3%A9l._Harck%C3%A9ptelenn%C3%A9_tett_ISU-152-es_szovjet_rohaml%C3%B6vegek,_a_h%C3%A1tt%C3%A9rben_egy_T-34-85_harckocsi._Fortepan_24854.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/J%C3%B3zsef_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_a_Corvin_%28Kisfaludy%29_k%C3%B6zn%C3%A9l._Harck%C3%A9ptelenn%C3%A9_tett_ISU-152-es_szovjet_rohaml%C3%B6vegek%2C_a_h%C3%A1tt%C3%A9rben_egy_T-34-85_harckocsi._Fortepan_24854.jpg/220px-J%C3%B3zsef_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_a_Corvin_%28Kisfaludy%29_k%C3%B6zn%C3%A9l._Harck%C3%A9ptelenn%C3%A9_tett_ISU-152-es_szovjet_rohaml%C3%B6vegek%2C_a_h%C3%A1tt%C3%A9rben_egy_T-34-85_harckocsi._Fortepan_24854.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/J%C3%B3zsef_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_a_Corvin_%28Kisfaludy%29_k%C3%B6zn%C3%A9l._Harck%C3%A9ptelenn%C3%A9_tett_ISU-152-es_szovjet_rohaml%C3%B6vegek%2C_a_h%C3%A1tt%C3%A9rben_egy_T-34-85_harckocsi._Fortepan_24854.jpg/330px-J%C3%B3zsef_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_a_Corvin_%28Kisfaludy%29_k%C3%B6zn%C3%A9l._Harck%C3%A9ptelenn%C3%A9_tett_ISU-152-es_szovjet_rohaml%C3%B6vegek%2C_a_h%C3%A1tt%C3%A9rben_egy_T-34-85_harckocsi._Fortepan_24854.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/J%C3%B3zsef_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_a_Corvin_%28Kisfaludy%29_k%C3%B6zn%C3%A9l._Harck%C3%A9ptelenn%C3%A9_tett_ISU-152-es_szovjet_rohaml%C3%B6vegek%2C_a_h%C3%A1tt%C3%A9rben_egy_T-34-85_harckocsi._Fortepan_24854.jpg/440px-J%C3%B3zsef_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_a_Corvin_%28Kisfaludy%29_k%C3%B6zn%C3%A9l._Harck%C3%A9ptelenn%C3%A9_tett_ISU-152-es_szovjet_rohaml%C3%B6vegek%2C_a_h%C3%A1tt%C3%A9rben_egy_T-34-85_harckocsi._Fortepan_24854.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4824" data-file-height="3293" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:J%C3%B3zsef_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_a_Corvin_(Kisfaludy)_k%C3%B6zn%C3%A9l._Harck%C3%A9ptelenn%C3%A9_tett_ISU-152-es_szovjet_rohaml%C3%B6vegek,_a_h%C3%A1tt%C3%A9rben_egy_T-34-85_harckocsi._Fortepan_24854.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Destroyed Soviet assault guns <a href="/wiki/ISU-152" title="ISU-152">ISU-152</a> in Budapest <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zsefv%C3%A1ros" title="Józsefváros">8th District</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Between 4 and 9 November, the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Ground_Forces" title="Hungarian Ground Forces">Hungarian Army</a> put up sporadic and disorganised resistance, with Marshal Zhukov reporting the disarming of twelve divisions, two armoured regiments, and the entire Hungarian Air Force. The Hungarian Army continued its most formidable resistance in various districts of Budapest and in and around the city of <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A9cs" title="Pécs">Pécs</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Mecsek" title="Mecsek">Mecsek Mountains</a>, and in the industrial centre of <a href="/wiki/Duna%C3%BAjv%C3%A1ros" title="Dunaújváros">Dunaújváros</a> (then called Stalintown). Fighting in Budapest consisted of between ten and fifteen thousand resistance fighters, with the heaviest fighting occurring in the working-class stronghold of Csepel on the Danube River.<sup id="cite_ref-Lindvai_2008_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lindvai_2008-143">[143]</a></sup> Although some very senior officers were openly pro-Soviet, the rank and file soldiers were overwhelmingly loyal to the revolution and either fought against the invasion or deserted. The United Nations reported that there were no recorded incidents of Hungarian Army units fighting on the side of the Soviets.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>At 05:20 on 4 November, Imre Nagy broadcast his final plea to the nation and the world, announcing that Soviet Forces were attacking Budapest and that the Government remained at its post.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The radio station, Free <a href="/wiki/Kossuth_R%C3%A1di%C3%B3" title="Kossuth Rádió">Kossuth Rádió</a>, stopped broadcasting at 08:07<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> An emergency Cabinet meeting was held in the Parliament but was attended by only three ministers. As Soviet troops arrived to occupy the building, a negotiated evacuation ensued, leaving Minister of State <a href="/wiki/Istv%C3%A1n_Bib%C3%B3" title="István Bibó">István Bibó</a> as the last representative of the National Government remaining at his post.<sup id="cite_ref-Bibo_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bibo-147">[147]</a></sup> He wrote <a href="/wiki/For_Freedom_and_Truth" title="For Freedom and Truth">For Freedom and Truth</a>, a <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/For_Freedom_and_Truth" class="extiw" title="wikisource:For Freedom and Truth">stirring proclamation</a> to the nation and the world.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Erzs%C3%A9bet_(Lenin)_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt,_a_Royal_Sz%C3%A1ll%C3%B3val_(ma_Corinthia_Hotel)_szemben._Fortepan_15317.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Erzs%C3%A9bet_%28Lenin%29_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt%2C_a_Royal_Sz%C3%A1ll%C3%B3val_%28ma_Corinthia_Hotel%29_szemben._Fortepan_15317.jpg/220px-Erzs%C3%A9bet_%28Lenin%29_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt%2C_a_Royal_Sz%C3%A1ll%C3%B3val_%28ma_Corinthia_Hotel%29_szemben._Fortepan_15317.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Erzs%C3%A9bet_%28Lenin%29_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt%2C_a_Royal_Sz%C3%A1ll%C3%B3val_%28ma_Corinthia_Hotel%29_szemben._Fortepan_15317.jpg/330px-Erzs%C3%A9bet_%28Lenin%29_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt%2C_a_Royal_Sz%C3%A1ll%C3%B3val_%28ma_Corinthia_Hotel%29_szemben._Fortepan_15317.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Erzs%C3%A9bet_%28Lenin%29_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt%2C_a_Royal_Sz%C3%A1ll%C3%B3val_%28ma_Corinthia_Hotel%29_szemben._Fortepan_15317.jpg/440px-Erzs%C3%A9bet_%28Lenin%29_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt%2C_a_Royal_Sz%C3%A1ll%C3%B3val_%28ma_Corinthia_Hotel%29_szemben._Fortepan_15317.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1688" data-file-height="1112" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Erzs%C3%A9bet_(Lenin)_k%C3%B6r%C3%BAt,_a_Royal_Sz%C3%A1ll%C3%B3val_(ma_Corinthia_Hotel)_szemben._Fortepan_15317.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Ruszkik haza!</i> (<i>Russians go home!</i>) slogan in Budapest</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At 06:00, on 4 November,<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> in the town of <a href="/wiki/Szolnok" title="Szolnok">Szolnok</a>, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1nos_K%C3%A1d%C3%A1r" title="János Kádár">János Kádár</a> proclaimed the "Hungarian Revolutionary Worker-Peasant Government". His statement declared "We must put an end to the excesses of the counter-revolutionary elements. The hour for action has sounded. We are going to defend the interest of the workers and peasants and the achievements of the people's democracy."<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Later that evening, Kádár called upon "the faithful fighters of the true cause of socialism" to come out of hiding and take up arms. However, Hungarian support did not materialise; the fighting did not take on the character of an internally divisive civil war, but rather, in the words of a United Nations report, that of "a well-equipped foreign army crushing by overwhelming force a national movement and eliminating the Government."<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%C3%9Cll%C5%91i_%C3%BAt_a_Nagyk%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_fel%C5%91l_a_Vajdahunyad_utca_fel%C3%A9_n%C3%A9zve._Fortepan_24768.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/%C3%9Cll%C5%91i_%C3%BAt_a_Nagyk%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_fel%C5%91l_a_Vajdahunyad_utca_fel%C3%A9_n%C3%A9zve._Fortepan_24768.jpg/220px-%C3%9Cll%C5%91i_%C3%BAt_a_Nagyk%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_fel%C5%91l_a_Vajdahunyad_utca_fel%C3%A9_n%C3%A9zve._Fortepan_24768.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/%C3%9Cll%C5%91i_%C3%BAt_a_Nagyk%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_fel%C5%91l_a_Vajdahunyad_utca_fel%C3%A9_n%C3%A9zve._Fortepan_24768.jpg/330px-%C3%9Cll%C5%91i_%C3%BAt_a_Nagyk%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_fel%C5%91l_a_Vajdahunyad_utca_fel%C3%A9_n%C3%A9zve._Fortepan_24768.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/%C3%9Cll%C5%91i_%C3%BAt_a_Nagyk%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_fel%C5%91l_a_Vajdahunyad_utca_fel%C3%A9_n%C3%A9zve._Fortepan_24768.jpg/440px-%C3%9Cll%C5%91i_%C3%BAt_a_Nagyk%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_fel%C5%91l_a_Vajdahunyad_utca_fel%C3%A9_n%C3%A9zve._Fortepan_24768.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4506" data-file-height="2920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%C3%9Cll%C5%91i_%C3%BAt_a_Nagyk%C3%B6r%C3%BAt_fel%C5%91l_a_Vajdahunyad_utca_fel%C3%A9_n%C3%A9zve._Fortepan_24768.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rubble after end of fighting in Budapest 8th District</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By 08:00 organised defence of the city evaporated after the radio station was seized, and many defenders fell back to fortified positions.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_report_5C_p197_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_report_5C_p197-152">[152]</a></sup> During the same hour, the parliamentary guard laid down their arms, and forces under Major General K. Grebennik captured Parliament and liberated captured ministers of the <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A1kosi" class="mw-redirect" title="Rákosi">Rákosi</a>-<a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A1s_Heged%C3%BCs" title="András Hegedüs">Hegedüs</a> government. Among the liberated were <a href="/wiki/Istv%C3%A1n_Dobi" title="István Dobi">István Dobi</a> and <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1ndor_R%C3%B3nai" title="Sándor Rónai">Sándor Rónai</a>, both of whom became members of the re-established socialist Hungarian government.<sup id="cite_ref-Lindvai_2008_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lindvai_2008-143">[143]</a></sup> Hungarian civilians bore the brunt of the fighting, as Soviet troops spared little effort to differentiate military from civilian targets.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> For this reason, Soviet tanks often crept along main roads firing indiscriminately into buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_report_5C_p197_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_report_5C_p197-152">[152]</a></sup> Hungarian resistance was strongest in the industrial areas of Budapest, which were heavily targeted by Soviet artillery and air strikes.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>The longest holdouts against the Soviet assault occurred in <a href="/wiki/Csepel" title="Csepel">Csepel</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Duna%C3%BAjv%C3%A1ros" title="Dunaújváros">Dunaújváros</a>, where fighting lasted until 11 November before the insurgents finally succumbed to the Soviets.<sup id="cite_ref-Lendvai_2008_61-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lendvai_2008-61">[61]</a></sup> At the end of the fighting, Hungarian casualties totalled at around 2,500 dead with an additional 20,000 wounded. Budapest bore the brunt of the bloodshed, with 1,569 civilians killed.<sup id="cite_ref-Lendvai_2008_61-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lendvai_2008-61">[61]</a></sup> Approximately 53 percent of the dead were workers, and about half of all the casualties were people younger than thirty. On the Soviet side, 699 men were killed, 1,450 men were wounded, and 51 men were missing in action. Estimates place around 80 percent of all casualties occurring in fighting with the insurgents in the eighth and ninth districts of Budapest.<sup id="cite_ref-Lendvai_2008_61-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lendvai_2008-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soviet_version_of_the_events">Soviet version of the events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Soviet version of the events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Soviet reports of the events surrounding, during, and after the disturbance were remarkably consistent in their accounts, more so after the Second Soviet intervention cemented support for the Soviet position among international Communist Parties. <a href="/wiki/Pravda" title="Pravda">Pravda</a> published an account 36 hours after the outbreak of violence, which set the tone for all further reports and subsequent Soviet historiography:<sup id="cite_ref-Barghoorn_SFP_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barghoorn_SFP-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li>On 23 October, the honest socialist Hungarians demonstrated against mistakes made by the <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A1ty%C3%A1s_R%C3%A1kosi" title="Mátyás Rákosi">Rákosi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ern%C5%91_Ger%C5%91" title="Ernő Gerő">Gerő</a> governments.</li>
<li>Fascist, Hitlerite, reactionary, counter-revolutionary hooligans financed by the imperialist west took advantage of the unrest to stage a counter-revolution.</li>
<li>The honest Hungarian people under Nagy appealed to Soviet (Warsaw Pact) forces stationed in Hungary to assist in restoring order.</li>
<li>The Nagy government was ineffective, allowing itself to be penetrated by counter-revolutionary influences, weakening then disintegrating, as proven by Nagy's culminating denouncement of the Warsaw Pact.</li>
<li>Hungarian patriots under <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1nos_K%C3%A1d%C3%A1r" title="János Kádár">Kádár</a> broke with the Nagy government and formed a government of honest Hungarian revolutionary workers and peasants; this genuinely popular government petitioned the Soviet command to help put down the counter-revolution.</li>
<li>Hungarian patriots, with Soviet assistance, smashed the counter-revolution.</li>
</ol>
<p>The first Soviet report came out 24 hours after the first Western report. Nagy's appeal to the United Nations was not reported. After Nagy was arrested outside of the Yugoslav embassy, his arrest was not reported. Nor did accounts explain how Nagy went from patriot to traitor.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> The Soviet press reported calm in Budapest while the Western press reported a revolutionary crisis was breaking out. According to the Soviet account, Hungarians never wanted a revolution at all.<sup id="cite_ref-Barghoorn_SFP_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barghoorn_SFP-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January 1957, representatives of the Soviet Union, Bulgaria, Hungary, and Romania met in Budapest to review internal developments in Hungary since the establishment of the Soviet-imposed government. A communiqué on the meeting "unanimously concluded" that Hungarian workers, with the leadership of the Kádár government and support of the Soviet army, defeated attempts "to eliminate the socialist achievements of the Hungarian people".<sup id="cite_ref-communique_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-communique-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>Soviet, Chinese, and other Warsaw Pact governments urged Kádár to proceed with interrogation and trial of former Nagy government ministers, and asked for punitive measures against the"counter-revolutionists".<sup id="cite_ref-communique_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-communique-159">[159]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> In addition the Kádár government published an extensive series of "white books" (<i>The Counter-Revolutionary Forces in the October Events in Hungary</i>) documenting real incidents of violence against Communist Party and ÁVH members, and the confessions of Nagy supporters. These white books were widely distributed in several languages in most of the socialist countries and, while based in fact, present factual evidence with a colouring and narrative not generally supported by non-Soviet aligned historians.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hungary">Hungary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Hungary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the immediate aftermath, many thousands of Hungarians were arrested. Eventually, 26,000 of these were brought before the Hungarian courts, 22,000 were sentenced, 13,000 imprisoned, and several hundred executed. Hundreds were also deported to the Soviet Union, many without evidence. Approximately 200,000<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> fled Hungary as refugees.<sup id="cite_ref-Fink_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fink-163">[163]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cseresneyes_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cseresneyes-165">[165]</a></sup> Former Hungarian Foreign Minister Géza Jeszenszky estimated 350 were executed.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-129">[129]</a></sup> Sporadic armed resistance and strikes by <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_council" title="Workers' council">workers' councils</a> continued until mid-1957, causing substantial economic disruption.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> By 1963, most <a href="/wiki/Political_prisoner" title="Political prisoner">political prisoners</a> from the 1956 Hungarian revolution had been released.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>With most of Budapest under Soviet control by 8 November, Kádár became Prime Minister of the "Revolutionary Worker-Peasant Government" and General Secretary of the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Communist_Party" title="Hungarian Communist Party">Hungarian Communist Party</a>. Few Hungarians rejoined the reorganised Party, its leadership having been purged under the supervision of the Soviet Praesidium, led by <a href="/wiki/Georgy_Malenkov" title="Georgy Malenkov">Georgy Malenkov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Suslov" title="Mikhail Suslov">Mikhail Suslov</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-situation_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-situation-168">[168]</a></sup> Although Party membership declined from 800,000 before the uprising to 100,000 by December 1956, Kádár steadily increased his control over Hungary and neutralised dissenters. The new government attempted to enlist support by espousing popular principles of Hungarian self-determination voiced during the uprising, but Soviet troops remained.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> After 1956 the Soviet Union severely purged the Hungarian Army and reinstituted political indoctrination in the units that remained. In May 1957, the Soviet Union increased its troop levels in Hungary and by treaty Hungary accepted the Soviet presence on a permanent basis.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Red_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Cross">Red Cross</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Austrian Army">Austrian Army</a> established refugee camps in <a href="/wiki/Baden_(district_of_Austria)" class="mw-redirect" title="Baden (district of Austria)">Traiskirchen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Graz" title="Graz">Graz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cseresneyes_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cseresneyes-165">[165]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Imre_Nagy" title="Imre Nagy">Imre Nagy</a> along with <a href="/wiki/Georg_Luk%C3%A1cs" class="mw-redirect" title="Georg Lukács">Georg Lukács</a>, <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9za_Losonczy" title="Géza Losonczy">Géza Losonczy</a>, and László Rajk's widow, Júlia, took refuge in the Embassy of Yugoslavia as Soviet forces overran Budapest. Despite assurances of safe passage out of Hungary by the Soviets and the Kádár government, Nagy and his group were arrested when attempting to leave the embassy on 22 November and taken to Romania. Losonczy died while on a hunger strike in prison awaiting trial when his jailers "carelessly pushed a feeding tube down his windpipe."<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></p>
<p>The remainder of the group was returned to Budapest in 1958. Nagy was executed, along with <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A1l_Mal%C3%A9ter" title="Pál Maléter">Pál Maléter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mikl%C3%B3s_Gimes" title="Miklós Gimes">Miklós Gimes</a>, after secret trials in June 1958. Their bodies were placed in unmarked graves in the <a href="/wiki/New_Public_Cemetery,_Budapest" title="New Public Cemetery, Budapest">Municipal Cemetery</a> outside Budapest.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCJune16_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCJune16-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the November 1956 Soviet assault on Budapest, <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_Mindszenty" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardinal Mindszenty">Cardinal Mindszenty</a> was granted political asylum at the United States embassy, where he lived for the next 15 years, refusing to leave Hungary unless the government reversed his 1949 conviction for treason. Because of poor health and a request from the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Vatican</a>, he finally left the embassy for Austria in September 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International">International</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: International">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Despite Cold War rhetoric by western countries espousing a roll-back of the domination of Europe by the USSR and Soviet promises of the imminent triumph of socialism, national leaders of this period as well as later historians saw the failure of the uprising in Hungary as evidence that the Cold War in Europe had become a stalemate.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Foreign Minister of West Germany recommended that the people of Eastern Europe be discouraged from "taking dramatic action which might have disastrous consequences for themselves." <a href="/wiki/Paul-Henri_Spaak" title="Paul-Henri Spaak">The Secretary-General of NATO</a> called the Hungarian revolt "the collective suicide of a whole people".<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> In a newspaper interview in 1957, Khrushchev commented "support by United States&#160;... is rather in the nature of the support that the rope gives to a hanged man."<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_the_Hungarian_Freedom_Fighters_at_Camp_Roeder_in_Salzburg,_Austria_-_NARA_-_195987.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_the_Hungarian_Freedom_Fighters_at_Camp_Roeder_in_Salzburg%2C_Austria_-_NARA_-_195987.jpg/220px-Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_the_Hungarian_Freedom_Fighters_at_Camp_Roeder_in_Salzburg%2C_Austria_-_NARA_-_195987.jpg" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_the_Hungarian_Freedom_Fighters_at_Camp_Roeder_in_Salzburg%2C_Austria_-_NARA_-_195987.jpg/330px-Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_the_Hungarian_Freedom_Fighters_at_Camp_Roeder_in_Salzburg%2C_Austria_-_NARA_-_195987.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_the_Hungarian_Freedom_Fighters_at_Camp_Roeder_in_Salzburg%2C_Austria_-_NARA_-_195987.jpg/440px-Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_the_Hungarian_Freedom_Fighters_at_Camp_Roeder_in_Salzburg%2C_Austria_-_NARA_-_195987.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2176" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_the_Hungarian_Freedom_Fighters_at_Camp_Roeder_in_Salzburg,_Austria_-_NARA_-_195987.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Roosevelt" title="Eleanor Roosevelt">Eleanor Roosevelt</a> meets exiled Hungarian revolutionaries at Camp Roeder in Salzburg, 10th May 1957</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In January 1957, United Nations Secretary-General <a href="/wiki/Dag_Hammarskj%C3%B6ld" title="Dag Hammarskjöld">Dag Hammarskjöld</a>, acting in response to UN General Assembly resolutions requesting investigation and observation of the events in Soviet-occupied Hungary, established the Special Committee on the Problem of Hungary.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> The Committee, with representatives from Australia, Ceylon (<a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>), Denmark, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguay</a>, conducted hearings in New York, Geneva, Rome, Vienna, and London. Over five months, 111 refugees were interviewed including ministers, military commanders and other officials of the Nagy government, workers, <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_council" class="mw-redirect" title="Revolutionary council">revolutionary council</a> members, factory managers and technicians, Communists and non-Communists, students, writers, teachers, medical personnel, and Hungarian soldiers. Documents, newspapers, radio transcripts, photos, film footage, and other records from Hungary were also reviewed, as well as written testimony of 200 other Hungarians.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup></p>
<p>The governments of Hungary and Romania refused the UN officials of the Committee entry, and the government of the Soviet Union did not respond to requests for information.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> The 268-page Committee Report<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> was presented to the General Assembly in June 1957, documenting the course of the uprising and Soviet intervention, and concluding that "the Kádár government and Soviet occupation were in violation of the human rights of the Hungarian people."<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> A General Assembly resolution was approved, deploring "the repression of the Hungarian people and the Soviet occupation" but no other action was taken.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> The chairman of the Committee was Alsing Andersen, a Danish politician and leading figure of Denmark's Social Democratic Party. He served in the Buhl government in 1942 during the Nazi German occupation of Denmark. He defended collaboration with the occupation forces and denounced the Resistance. He was appointed Interior Minister in 1947, but resigned because of scrutiny of his role in 1940 as Defence Minister. He then entered Denmark's UN delegation in 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Committee Report and the motives of its authors were criticised by delegations to the United Nations. The Hungarian representative disagreed with the report's conclusions, accusing it of falsifying the events, and argued that the establishment of the Committee was illegal. The Committee was accused of being hostile to Hungary and its social system.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> An article in the Russian journal "International Affairs", published by the Foreign Affairs Ministry, carried an article in 1957 in which it denounced the report as a "collection of falsehoods and distortions".<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine named the Hungarian Freedom Fighter its <a href="/wiki/Time_Person_of_the_Year" title="Time Person of the Year">Man of the Year</a> for 1956. The accompanying <i>Time</i> article comments that this choice could not have been anticipated until the explosive events of the revolution, almost at the end of 1956. The magazine cover and accompanying text displayed an artist's depiction of a Hungarian freedom fighter, and used pseudonyms for the three participants whose stories are the subject of the article.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2006, Hungarian Prime Minister Ferenc Gyurcsány referred to this famous <i>Time</i> Man of the Year cover as "the faces of free Hungary" in a speech to mark the 50th anniversary of the 1956 uprising.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> Prime Minister Gyurcsány, in a joint appearance with British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>, commented specifically on the Time cover itself, that "It is an idealised image but the faces of the figures are really the face of the revolutionaries"<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/1956_Summer_Olympics" title="1956 Summer Olympics">Melbourne Olympics</a> in 1956, the Soviet handling of the Hungarian uprising led to a boycott by Spain, the Netherlands, and Switzerland.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> At the Olympic Village, the Hungarian delegation tore down the Communist Hungarian flag and raised the flag of Free Hungary in its place. A confrontation between Soviet and Hungarian teams occurred in the <a href="/wiki/Water_polo_at_the_1956_Summer_Olympics" title="Water polo at the 1956 Summer Olympics">semi-final match</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Water_polo" title="Water polo">water polo</a> tournament on 6 December. The match was extremely violent, and was halted in the final minute to quell fighting among spectators. This match, now known as the "<a href="/wiki/Blood_In_The_Water_match" class="mw-redirect" title="Blood In The Water match">blood in the water match</a>", became the subject of several films.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> The Hungarian team won the game 4–0 and later was awarded the Olympic gold medal. In 1957, <a href="/wiki/Norway_national_bandy_team" title="Norway national bandy team">Norway</a> was invited to the first ever <a href="/wiki/1957_Bandy_World_Championship" title="1957 Bandy World Championship">Bandy World Championship</a> but declined because the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union_national_bandy_team" title="Soviet Union national bandy team">Soviet Union</a> was invited too.</p>
<p>On Sunday, 28 October 1956, as some 55 million Americans watched <a href="/wiki/Ed_Sullivan" title="Ed Sullivan">Ed Sullivan</a>'s popular television <a href="/wiki/Variety_show" title="Variety show">variety show</a>, with the then 21-year-old <a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a> headlining for the second time, Sullivan asked viewers to send aid to Hungarian refugees fleeing from the effects of the Soviet invasion. Presley himself made another request for donations during his third and last appearance on Sullivan's show on 6 January 1957. Presley then dedicated a song for the finale, which he thought fit the mood of the time, namely the gospel song "<a href="/wiki/Peace_in_the_Valley" title="Peace in the Valley">Peace in the Valley</a>". By the end of 1957, these contributions, distributed by the Geneva-based <a href="/wiki/International_Red_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="International Red Cross">International Red Cross</a> as food rations, clothing, and other essentials, had amounted to some <a href="/wiki/Swiss_franc" title="Swiss franc">SFR</a> 26 million (US$6 million in 1957 dollars), the equivalent of $50,600,000 in today's dollars.<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-US_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-194">[194]</a></sup> On 1 March 2011, <a href="/wiki/Istv%C3%A1n_Tarl%C3%B3s" title="István Tarlós">István Tarlós</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_Budapest" title="Mayor of Budapest">Mayor of Budapest</a>, made Presley an honorary citizen, posthumously, and a plaza located at the intersection of two of the city's most important avenues was named after Presley, as a gesture of gratitude.</p>
<p>Meanwhile, as the 1950s drew to a close, the events in Hungary produced ideological fractures within the Communist parties of Western Europe. Within the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Communist_Party" title="Italian Communist Party">Italian Communist Party</a> (PCI) a split ensued: most ordinary members and the Party leadership, including <a href="/wiki/Palmiro_Togliatti" title="Palmiro Togliatti">Palmiro Togliatti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Napolitano" title="Giorgio Napolitano">Giorgio Napolitano</a>, regarded the Hungarian insurgents as counter-revolutionaries, as reported in <i><a href="/wiki/L%27Unit%C3%A0" title="L'Unità">l'Unità</a></i>, the official PCI newspaper.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> However <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Di_Vittorio" title="Giuseppe Di Vittorio">Giuseppe Di Vittorio</a>, chief of the Communist trade union <a href="/wiki/CGIL" class="mw-redirect" title="CGIL">CGIL</a>, repudiated the leadership position, as did the prominent party members <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Giolitti" title="Antonio Giolitti">Antonio Giolitti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Loris_Fortuna" title="Loris Fortuna">Loris Fortuna</a>, and many other influential Communist intellectuals, who later were expelled or left the party. <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Nenni" title="Pietro Nenni">Pietro Nenni</a>, the national secretary of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Socialist_Party" title="Italian Socialist Party">Italian Socialist Party</a>, a close ally of the PCI, opposed the Soviet intervention as well. Napolitano, elected in 2006 as <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Italian_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="President of the Italian Republic">President of the Italian Republic</a>, wrote in his 2005 political autobiography that he regretted his justification of Soviet action in Hungary, and that at the time he believed in Party unity and the international leadership of Soviet communism.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup></p>
<p>Within the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Great_Britain" title="Communist Party of Great Britain">Communist Party of Great Britain</a> (CPGB), dissent that began with the repudiation of Stalin by <a href="/wiki/John_Saville" title="John Saville">John Saville</a> and <a href="/wiki/E._P._Thompson" title="E. P. Thompson">E. P. Thompson</a>, influential historians and members of the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_Historians_Group" title="Communist Party Historians Group">Communist Party Historians Group</a>, culminated in a loss of thousands of party members as events unfolded in Hungary. <a href="/wiki/Peter_Fryer" title="Peter Fryer">Peter Fryer</a>, correspondent for the CPGB newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Morning_Star_(UK_newspaper)" class="mw-redirect" title="Morning Star (UK newspaper)">The Daily Worker</a></i>, reported accurately on the violent suppression of the uprising, but his dispatches were heavily censored.<sup id="cite_ref-Fryer_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fryer-140">[140]</a></sup> Fryer resigned from the paper upon his return, and was later expelled from the Communist Party</p>
<p>In France, moderate Communists, such as historian <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel_Le_Roy_Ladurie" title="Emmanuel Le Roy Ladurie">Emmanuel Le Roy Ladurie</a>, resigned, questioning the <a href="/wiki/French_Communist_Party" title="French Communist Party">French Communist Party</a>'s policy of supporting Soviet actions. The French philosopher and writer <a href="/wiki/Albert_Camus" title="Albert Camus">Albert Camus</a> wrote an <a href="/wiki/Open_letter" title="Open letter">open letter</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Blood_of_the_Hungarians" class="mw-redirect" title="The Blood of the Hungarians">The Blood of the Hungarians</a></i>, criticising the West's lack of action. Even <a href="/wiki/Jean-Paul_Sartre" title="Jean-Paul Sartre">Jean-Paul Sartre</a>, still a determined Communist, criticised the Soviets in his article <i>Le Fantôme de Staline</i>, in <i>Situations VII</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Left_Communists" class="mw-redirect" title="Left Communists">Left Communists</a> were particularly supportive of the revolution.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemoration">Commemoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Commemoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Budape%C5%A1%C5%A5,_H%C3%B6s%C3%B6k_t%C3%A9re,_pam%C4%9Btn%C3%AD_deska_1956.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Budape%C5%A1%C5%A5%2C_H%C3%B6s%C3%B6k_t%C3%A9re%2C_pam%C4%9Btn%C3%AD_deska_1956.jpg/220px-Budape%C5%A1%C5%A5%2C_H%C3%B6s%C3%B6k_t%C3%A9re%2C_pam%C4%9Btn%C3%AD_deska_1956.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Budape%C5%A1%C5%A5%2C_H%C3%B6s%C3%B6k_t%C3%A9re%2C_pam%C4%9Btn%C3%AD_deska_1956.jpg/330px-Budape%C5%A1%C5%A5%2C_H%C3%B6s%C3%B6k_t%C3%A9re%2C_pam%C4%9Btn%C3%AD_deska_1956.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Budape%C5%A1%C5%A5%2C_H%C3%B6s%C3%B6k_t%C3%A9re%2C_pam%C4%9Btn%C3%AD_deska_1956.jpg/440px-Budape%C5%A1%C5%A5%2C_H%C3%B6s%C3%B6k_t%C3%A9re%2C_pam%C4%9Btn%C3%AD_deska_1956.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4770" data-file-height="3178" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Budape%C5%A1%C5%A5,_H%C3%B6s%C3%B6k_t%C3%A9re,_pam%C4%9Btn%C3%AD_deska_1956.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Memorial plaque at the <a href="/wiki/Embassy_of_Serbia,_Budapest" title="Embassy of Serbia, Budapest">Embassy of Serbia, Budapest</a> in memory of <a href="/wiki/Imre_Nagy" title="Imre Nagy">Imre Nagy</a> who found a sanctuary there during the Hungarian Revolution of 1956</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1956flag.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Tricolor Hungarian flag with circle cut from the middle flying atop a bent flagpole in front of a large Neogothic building" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/1956flag.jpg/250px-1956flag.jpg" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/1956flag.jpg/375px-1956flag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/1956flag.jpg/500px-1956flag.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1956flag.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1956 Revolution Flag flying in front of the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Parliament_Building" title="Hungarian Parliament Building">Hungarian Parliament Building</a>, 2006</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In December 1991, the preamble of the treaties with the dismembered Soviet Union, under <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a>, and Russia, represented by <a href="/wiki/Boris_Yeltsin" title="Boris Yeltsin">Boris Yeltsin</a>, apologised officially for the 1956 Soviet actions in Hungary. This apology was repeated by Yeltsin in 1992 during a speech to the Hungarian parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn_129-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 13 February 2006, the <a href="/wiki/US_State_Department" class="mw-redirect" title="US State Department">US State Department</a> commemorated the fiftieth anniversary of the 1956 Hungarian Revolution. Former US Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Condoleezza_Rice" title="Condoleezza Rice">Rice</a> commented on the contributions made by 1956 Hungarian refugees to the United States and other host countries, as well as the <a href="/wiki/German_reunification" title="German reunification">role of Hungary</a> in providing refuge to East Germans during the 1989 protests against Communist rule.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/United_States_of_America" class="mw-redirect" title="United States of America">US</a> President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> also visited Hungary on 22 June 2006, to commemorate the fiftieth anniversary.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 16 June 1989, the 31st anniversary of his execution, Imre Nagy's body was reburied with full honours.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCJune16_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCJune16-173">[173]</a></sup> The Republic of Hungary was declared in 1989 on the 33rd anniversary of the Revolution, and 23 October is now a Hungarian <a href="/wiki/Public_holiday" title="Public holiday">national holiday</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the north-west corner of <a href="/wiki/MacArthur_Park" title="MacArthur Park">MacArthur Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles,_California" class="mw-redirect" title="Los Angeles, California">Los Angeles, California</a>, the Hungarian-American community built a commemorative statue to honour the Hungarian freedom fighters. Built in the late 1960s, the obelisk statue stands with an American eagle watching over the city of Los Angeles.</p>

<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sources vary widely on numbers of Soviet forces involved in the intervention. The UN General Assembly <i>Special Committee on the Problem of Hungary</i> (1957) estimated 75,000–200,000 troops and 1,600–4,000 tanks <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://mek.oszk.hu/01200/01274/01274.pdf">OSZK.hu</a> (p. 56, para. 183), but recently released Soviet archives (available in <a href="/wiki/Lib.ru" title="Lib.ru">Lib.ru</a>, Maksim Moshkow's Library) list the troop strength of the Soviet forces as 31,550, with 1,130 tanks and <a href="/wiki/Self-propelled_artillery" title="Self-propelled artillery">self-propelled artillery</a> pieces. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://fan.lib.ru/c/chekmarew_w_a/text_0200.shtml">Lib.ru</a> <span class="languageicon" style="font-size:0.95em; font-weight:bold; color:#555;">(Russian)</span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Györkei, Jenõ; Kirov, Alexandr; Horvath, Miklos (1999). <i>Soviet Military Intervention in Hungary, 1956</i>. New York: Central European University Press. p.&#160;370 table 111. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/963-9116-35-1" title="Special:BookSources/963-9116-35-1">963-9116-35-1</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHungarian+Revolution+of+1956&amp;rft.aufirst=Jen%C3%B5&amp;rft.au=Horvath%2C+Miklos&amp;rft.au=Kirov%2C+Alexandr&amp;rft.aulast=Gy%C3%B6rkei&amp;rft.btitle=Soviet+Military+Intervention+in+Hungary%2C+1956&amp;rft.date=1999&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=963-9116-35-1&amp;rft.pages=370+table+111&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Central+European+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-UNchVnote8-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-UNchVnote8_3-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">UN General Assembly <i>Special Committee on the Problem of Hungary</i> (1957) <span class="PDFlink"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://mek.oszk.hu/01200/01274/01274.pdf">Chapter V footnote 8</a></span><span style="font-size:smaller;">&#160;PDF&#160;(1.47&#160;MB)</span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"B&amp;J": Jacob Bercovitch and Richard Jackson, International Conflict&#160;: A Chronological Encyclopedia of Conflicts and Their Management 1945–1995 (1997)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Alternate references are "Hungarian Revolt" and "Hungarian Uprising". In Hungarian, first the neutral term "felkelés" (uprising) was used, then in the 1957–1988 period, the negative term "ellenforradalom" (counter-revolution) was mandated by the government, while the new official name after 1990 has become the positive "forradalom és szabadságharc" (revolution and freedom fight) to imitate the old expression for the 1848–1849 revolution. Another explanation of the terms is that "Revolution" conforms to both English <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.state.gov/r/pa/ei/bgn/26566.htm">(see US Department of State background on Hungary)</a> and Hungarian ("forradalom") conventions. There is a distinction between the "complete overthrow" of a revolution and an uprising or revolt that may or may not be successful (<a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a>). The 1956 Hungarian event, although short-lived, is a true "revolution" in that the sitting government was deposed. Unlike the terms "coup d'état" and "putsch" that imply action of a few, the 1956 revolution was initiated by the masses.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Kertesz, Stephen D. <li id="cite_note-lawsoc-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-lawsoc_16-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lawsoc_16-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Gati, Charles (September 2006). <i>Failed Illusions: Moscow, Washington, Budapest and the 1956 Hungarian Revolt</i>. Stanford University Press. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-8047-5606-6" title="Special:BookSources/0-8047-5606-6">0-8047-5606-6</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHungarian+Revolution+of+1956&amp;rft.aufirst=Charles&amp;rft.aulast=Gati&amp;rft.btitle=Failed+Illusions%3A+Moscow%2C+Washington%2C+Budapest+and+the+1956+Hungarian+Revolt&amp;rft.date=2006-09&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-8047-5606-6&amp;rft.pub=Stanford+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> (page 49). Gati describes "the most gruesome forms of psychological and physical torture&#160;... The reign of terror (by the Rákosi government) turned out to be harsher and more extensive than it was in any of the other Soviet satellites in Central and Eastern Europe." He further references a report prepared after the collapse of communism, the Fact Finding Commission <i>Törvénytelen szocializmus</i> (Lawless Socialism): "Between 1950 and early 1953, the courts dealt with 650,000 cases (of political crimes), of whom 387,000 or 4 percent of the population were found guilty. (Budapest, Zrínyi Kiadó/Új Magyarország, 1991, 154).</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Bibo-147"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Bibo_147-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Bibó, István (1991). <i>Democracy, Revolution, Self-Determination</i>. New York: Columbia University Press. pp.&#160;325–327. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-88033-214-X" title="Special:BookSources/0-88033-214-X">0-88033-214-X</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHungarian+Revolution+of+1956&amp;rft.aufirst=Istv%C3%A1n&amp;rft.aulast=Bib%C3%B3&amp;rft.btitle=Democracy%2C+Revolution%2C+Self-Determination&amp;rft.date=1991&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-88033-214-X&amp;rft.pages=325-327&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Columbia+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-148"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-148">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Istv%C3%A1n_Bib%C3%B3" title="István Bibó">Bibó, István</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://mek.niif.hu/02000/02043/html/524.html">"Nyilatkozat, 1956. November 4." [Declaration, November 4, 1956]</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://mek.niif.hu/02000/02043/html/index.html"><i>Válogatott tanulmányok</i> [<i>Assorted studies</i>]</a> (in Hungarian). 4, 1935–1979. ifj. István Bibó, Tibor Huszár<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2009-10-30</span></span>. <q>in Hungarian: Magyarok! Nagy Imre miniszterelnök a ma hajnali szovjet támadáskor a szovjet követségre ment a tárgyalások folytatására, és onnan visszatérni már nem tudott. A reggel összehívott minisztertanácson a Parlament épületében tartózkodó Tildy Zoltánon kívül már csak B. Szabó István és Bibó István államminiszter tudott megérkezni. Mikor a Parlamentet a szovjet csapatok körülfogták, Tildy államminiszter a vérontás elkerülése végett megállapodást kötött velük, mely szerint ők megszállják az épületet, a benne levő polgári személyek pedig szabadon távozhatnak. Ő, a megállapodáshoz tartva magát, eltávozott. Az országgyűlés épületében egyedül alulírott Bibó István államminiszter maradtam, mint az egyedüli törvényes magyar kormány egyedüli képviselője. Ebben a helyzetben a következőket nyilatkozom: In English: To My Fellow Hungarians! When the Soviet Army attacked today at dawn, Prime Minister Nagy Imre went to the Soviet Embassy to negotiate and could not return. Tildy Zoltán, who was already in the Parliament building, and ministers Szabó István and Bibó István attended the council of ministers meeting that was convened this morning. As Soviet troops surrounded the Parliament building, minister Tildy Zoltán, to avoid bloodshed, reached an agreement, by which Soviet soldiers would occupy the Parliament building and allow all civilians to evacuate. According to this agreement, he then departed. Only the undersigned, Bibó István, remained in the Psrliament building as the only representative of the only existing legal Hungarian government. Under these circumstances, I make the following declaration: (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/For_Freedom_and_Truth" class="extiw" title="s:For Freedom and Truth">Available in English</a>)</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHungarian+Revolution+of+1956&amp;rft.atitle=Nyilatkozat%2C+1956.+November+4.&amp;rft.aufirst=Istv%C3%A1n&amp;rft.aulast=Bib%C3%B3&amp;rft.btitle=V%C3%A1logatott+tanulm%C3%A1nyok&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fmek.niif.hu%2F02000%2F02043%2Fhtml%2F524.html&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-149"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-149">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">UN General Assembly <i>Special Committee on the Problem of Hungary</i> (1957) <span class="PDFlink"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://mek.oszk.hu/01200/01274/01274.pdf">Chapter VII.E, para 296 (p. 90)</a></span><span style="font-size:smaller;">&#160;PDF&#160;(1.47&#160;MB)</span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-150">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">UN General Assembly <i>Special Committee on the Problem of Hungary</i> (1957) <span class="PDFlink"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://mek.oszk.hu/01200/01274/01274.pdf">Chapter VIII.B, para 596 (p. 185)</a></span><span style="font-size:smaller;">&#160;PDF&#160;(1.47&#160;MB)</span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Barghoorn_SFP-157"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Barghoorn_SFP_157-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Barghoorn_SFP_157-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Barghoorn, Frederick. <i>Soviet Foreign Propaganda</i>. Princeton University Press. 1964.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-158"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-158">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Pravda (Moscow), 4 November [227/228]: "Without the Slightes Delays", Moscow<br />
Imre Nagy turned out to be, objectively speaking, an accomplice of the reactionary forces. Imre Nagy cannot and does not want to fight the dark forces of reaction&#160;... The Soviet Government, seeing that the presence of Soviet troops in Budapest might lead to further aggravation of the situation, ordered troops to leave Budapest, but ensuing events have shown that reactionary forces, taking advantage of the non-intervention of the Nagy Cabinet, have gone still further&#160;... The task of barring the way to reaction in Hungary has to be carried out without the slightest delay -such is the course dictated by events&#160;...<br />
<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.hungarian-history.hu/lib/revolt/rev16.htm">Retrieved 2007-10-8 Hungarian-history.hu</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-communique-159"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-communique_159-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-communique_159-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">George Washington University: The National Security Archive, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.gwu.edu/~nsarchiv/NSAEBB/NSAEBB76/doc11.pdf">Communiqué on the Meeting of Representatives of the Governments and the Communist and Workers' Parties of Bulgaria, Czechoslovakia, Hungary, Romania and the Soviet Union</a> (Budapest, 6 January 1957), Retrieved 2008-12-7</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-160"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-160">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">George Washington University: The National Security Archive, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.gwu.edu/%7Ensarchiv/NSAEBB/NSAEBB76/doc12.pdf">Minutes of the Meeting between the Hungarian and Chinese Delegations</a> in Budapest on 16 January 1957, Retrieved 2008-12-7</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-161"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-161">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>The 1956 Hungarian Revolution: A History in Documents</i> by Csaba Békés &amp; Malcolm Byrne (Published by Central European University Press, 2002, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9639241660" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 963-9241-66-0</a>, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9789639241664" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-963-9241-66-4</a>, 598 pages), p. 375, para 4: "...&#160;the (Kádár) regime had to find an explanation for the revolution and collapse of the old regime in October 1956&#160;... they chose to interpret the uprising as a conspiracy by anti-communist, reactionary forces. This is why they labeled many ordinary citizens' actions as crimes. Critical opposition attitudes were described as "a plot to overthrow the people's democratic regime", and workers and peasants who took part in the revolt were called "jailbirds, ragamuffins, and kulaks." Armed resistance to occupying forces became "murder and wrecking state property." This kind of terminology became part of the official ideology of the regime toward the outside world." Also p. 375, footnote 40: "For a typical survey of propaganda intended for distribution abroad, see the so called "White Books" entitled <i>The Counter-Revolutionary Forces in the October Events in Hungary</i>, 4 vols., (Budapest: Information Bureau of the Council of Ministers of the Hungarian People's Republic, 1956–1957)&#160;... The White Books published in the individual counties of Hungary in 1957–1958 summarized local "counter-revolutionary" events."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-175"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-175">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Johns Hopkins University Professor Charles Gati, in his book <i>Failed Illusions: Moscow, Washington, Budapest, and the 1956 Hungarian Revolt</i> (see Further reading, below), agreed with a 2002 essay by Hungarian historian Csaba Bekes, "Could the Hungarian Revolution Have Been Victorious in 1956?". Gati states: "Washington implicitly acknowledging the division of the continent into two camps, understood that Moscow would not let go of a country bordering on neutral but pro-Western Austria and an independent Yugoslavia, so it shed&#160;... tears over Soviet brutality, and exploited the propaganda opportunities&#160;..." (p. 208)</span></li>

<ul>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legacy.wilsoncenter.org/va2/index.cfm?topic_id=1409&amp;fuseaction=home.browse&amp;sort=collection&amp;item=1956%20Hungarian%20Revolution">1956 Hungarian Revolution Collection</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson_International_Center_for_Scholars" title="Woodrow Wilson International Center for Scholars">Woodrow Wilson International Center for Scholars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cold_War_International_History_Project" title="Cold War International History Project">Cold War International History Project</a> (Virtual Archive 2.0), containing documents and other source materials relating to the 1956 Revolution.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.rev.hu/">Institute of Revolutionary History, Hungary</a> A Hungarian language site providing historical photos and documents, books and reviews, and links to English language sites.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.osa.ceu.hu/digitalarchive/av/index.html#">OSA Digital Archive</a> Videos of the 1956 Hungarian Revolution</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/1956_Hungarian_Revolution_as_Depicted_in_Newsreels"><i>Universal Pictures and Warner Pathé newsreels regarding the revolution</i></a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://news.bbc.co.uk/onthisday/hi/dates/stories/november/4/newsid_2739000/2739039.stm">"On this day 4 November 1956: Soviet troops overrun Hungary"</a> (Accessed 12 October 2006) – BBC reports on the first day of the second Soviet intervention and the fall of the Nagy government.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://libcom.org/library/hungary-56-andy-anderson">Hungary '56</a> Andy Anderson's pamphlet, written in 1964 and originally published by <a href="/wiki/Solidarity_(UK)" title="Solidarity (UK)">Solidarity (UK)</a>, about events of the Hungarian uprising of 1956, focusing on Hungarian demands for economic and political self-management. (AK Press 2002, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0934868018" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 0-934868-01-8</a>)</li>
<li>The short film <i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/gov.archives.arc.2569633">Big Picture: Operation Mercy</a></i> is available for free download at the <a href="/wiki/Internet_Archive" title="Internet Archive">Internet Archive</a></li>
<li>The short film <i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/gov.archives.arc.1683326">Hungarian Revolution Aftermath (1956)</a></i> is available for free download at the <a href="/wiki/Internet_Archive" title="Internet Archive">Internet Archive</a></li>
<li>The short film <i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/gov.archives.arc.2049945">Hungarian Revolution (1956)</a></i> is available for free download at the <a href="/wiki/Internet_Archive" title="Internet Archive">Internet Archive</a></li>
</ul>

<dl>
<dt>Feature films</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.rferl.org/featuresarticle/2006/05/0e5164bb-fd53-4562-88c5-9aac69e5845c.html"><i>Freedom's Fury</i></a> The 2005 documentary film depicting events surrounding the Hungarian-Soviet confrontation in the Olympic water polo tournament, now known as the "blood in the water match". Narrated by Mark Spitz, produced by Lucy Liu and Quentin Tarantino.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0468559/"><i>Torn from the flag</i></a> Documentary film 2007. The significant global effects of the Hungarian revolution of 1956.</li>
</ul>

<dt>Commemorations</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.hungary1956.com/">The 1956 Portal</a> A resource for Hungarian-American organizations to highlight and promote their 1956 Hungarian Revolution commemoration activities, including 1956 photos, videos, resources, and events across the US.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.freedomfighter56.com/en_stories_2all.html">Freedom Fighter 56</a> Personal stories of survival and escape from participants in the revolution</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://multiculturalcanada.ca/node/1521">1956 Hungarian Memorial Oral History Project</a> Multicultural Canada oral history collection of revolution refugees in Canada</li>
</ul>
